The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
The Five Universals: Building Blocks of Definition
Author enumerates and defines the five predicables (universals) used in definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define each of the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams) and distinguish between essential and accidental predicates in Islamic definitions.

Core Idea
The five universals are the lenses through which every definition in Islamic sciences is evaluated.

This passage from al-Razi lays out the five types of universal predicates used in constructing definitions. Understanding these five categories is essential for anyone who wants to analyse definitions in fiqh, kalam, or logic. We will walk through each universal, its role in answering 'What is it?', and how to tell them apart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal as either part of the quiddity or external to it
The passage first distinguishes between predicates that are intrinsic to the quiddity (dhati) and those that are extrinsic. Look for phrases like 'دال على جزء الماهية' (indicator of a part of the quiddity) versus 'الدال على الخارج عن الماهية' (indicator of what is external to the quiddity).
2
If part of the quiddity, determine if it is the common part (genus) or the distinguishing part (differentia)
The common part shared by many species is the genus (jins), e.g., 'animal'. The part that distinguishes one species from others is the differentia (fasl), e.g., 'rational'. The passage says genus answers 'What is it?' by pure commonality, while differentia distinguishes.
3
If external, determine if it is necessary (property) or non-necessary (accident)
Extrinsic predicates are either necessary (lazim) or non-necessary. If necessary, it is a property (khassa) like the ability to laugh for humans. If non-necessary, it is a general accident ('arad 'amm) like 'standing'.
The passage also mentions property can be self-evident or not.
4
Recognize the relative hierarchy of genus and species
The passage explains that the same term can be a genus relative to lower species and a species relative to higher genera. For example, 'animal' is genus for 'human' but species for 'living being'. The highest genus is 'jins al-ajnas' and the lowest species is 'naw' al-anwa'.
5
Apply to a classical Islamic definition
Take the definition of 'human' in Islamic philosophy: 'rational animal'. Identify 'animal' as genus, 'rational' as differentia, 'capable of laughter' as property, and 'walking' as accident. This confirms the categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقولية في جواب ما هو بالخصوصية المحضة أو عامة ماهية أشخاص. وهي إما أن تكون مختلفة في شيء من الذاتيات فيكون تمام الجزء المشترك جنسا مقولا في جواب ما هو حسب الشركة المحضة أو لا يكون وهو المقول في جواب ما هو حسب الشركة والخصوصية معا. أما حسب الخصوصية فظاهر، وأما حسب الشركة فلأن كل ما لكل واحد منها من الذاتيات حاصل للآخر وإلا فلو اختص بعضها بما ليس للآخر لكان الاختلاف بينه وبين ذلك الآخر ليس بالعدد فقط بل بالذاتيات وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك، هذا خلف. فإذا كان تمام ما لكل واحد منها من الذاتيات مشتركا بينه وبين غيره فلأجل ذلك كان ذكره جوابا عن السؤال عن ماهية تلك الأشياء حسب الشركة والخصوصية. ثم إن الدال على جزء الماهية فقط إما أن يكون على كمال الجزء الذي به يشارك غيره وهو الجنس، وهو المقول في جواب ما هو حسب الشركة المحضة، أو على كمال الجزء الذي به يتميز عن غيره وهو الفصل، أو لا يكون واحدا من المذكورين وسيأتي بيان ذلك إن شاء الله تعالى. ثم إن الجنس قد يشتمل على غيره من الأجناس تحته فيكون نوعا بالنسبة إليه ثم الأجناس قد تترتب متصاعدة لا إلى نهاية لكن إلى ما لا جنس فوقه وهو جنس الأجناس، والأنواع قد تترتب متنازلة إلى ما لا نوع تحته وهو نوع الأنواع. والفصل قد يشارك غيره في الاضافة كالجنس. ثم إن الدال على الخارج عن الماهية إما أن يكون لازما أو غير لازم، واللازم إما أن يكون لازما للماهية أو للوجود. الأول إما أن يكون بينا لذاته كالخاصية للأناة أو لا يكون. فذلك الخاصية والعرض العام.
The predicable is in answer to 'What is it?' with specific particularity or generality of the quiddity of individuals. And it is either different in something of the essentials, so the complete common part is a genus said in answer to 'What is it?' according to pure commonality, or it is not, and it is said in answer to 'What is it?' according to commonality and particularity together. As for according to particularity, it is obvious; as for according to commonality, because everything that belongs to each one of them from the essentials belongs to the other, otherwise if some were specific with what the other does not have, the difference between it and that other would be not only in number but in essentials, and we have assumed it is not so; this is a contradiction. So when the complete of what belongs to each one of them from the essentials is shared between it and other, therefore its mention is an answer to the question about the quiddity of those things according to commonality and particularity. Then the indicator of a part of the quiddity only is either the complete part by which it participates with others, which is the genus, and it is said in answer to 'What is it?' according to pure commonality, or the complete part by which it is distinguished from others, which is the differentia, or it is not one of the mentioned; and its explanation will come, God willing. Then the genus may include others of genera under it, so it becomes a species relative to it; then genera may ascend in a hierarchy without end but to that which has no genus above it, which is the genus of genera, and species may descend to that which has no species under it, which is the species of species. The differentia may share with others in relation like the genus. Then the indicator of that which is external to the quiddity is either necessary or not necessary; the necessary is either necessary for the quiddity or for existence. The first is either self-evident like the property of humanity or not. That is the property and the general accident.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams) which are the building blocks of definition. The author starts by explaining that any quiddity is either particular (juz'i) or universal (kulli). The universal is then analyzed into genus, species, differentia, property, and general accident. He gives criteria: if the common part is the complete essence shared, it's genus; if the distinguishing part, it's differentia. Then he discusses hierarchies: genus of genera (jins al-ajnas) and species of species (naw' al-anwa'). Finally, he distinguishes between property (khassa) and general accident ('arad 'amm) based on necessity and relationship to quiddity. For Islamic example: 'animal' is genus for 'human'; 'rational' is differentia; 'capable of laughter' is a property; 'walking' is a general accident.

Key Takeaway
The five universals provide a complete toolbox for analysing any definition in the Islamic sciences.

?

Species and Genus as Answers to 'What Is It?'
Author defines species and genus as answers to 'ma huwa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between species and genus as answers to 'ma huwa' and identify which part of the quiddity each represents.

Core Idea
Species answers with particularity and commonality; genus with commonality alone.

In Islamic logic, defining a thing requires answering the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?). The answer reveals the quiddity (mahiyya). This lesson unpacks how species (naw') and genus (jins) function as parts of that answer, laying the groundwork for precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The passage focuses on the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?). Species and genus are defined as types of answers to this question.
This is the fundamental context for all definitions.
2
Distinguish particular vs common
Notice the two ways of answering: with pure particularity (for a single person) or commonality (for many). The passage explains that the answer can be particular, common, or both.
3
Define species
Species is the quiddity of a single person or the common quiddity of persons. It is said in answer to 'ma huwa' with particularity or with commonality and particularity together.
Species includes both the common and the distinguishing aspects.
4
Define genus
Genus is the part of the quiddity by which a thing participates with others. It is said in answer to 'ma huwa' according to common participation alone.
Genus does not include the distinguishing part.
5
Connect to differentia
The passage also introduces differentia (fasl) as the other essential part. A complete definition may combine genus and differentia to capture the full quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماهية شخص واحد وهو المقول في جواب ما هو بالخصوصية المحضة او عا ما هية اشخاص وهي اما ان تكون مختلفة ع شي من الذاتيات فيكون تمام الجزء المشترك سینا مقولاً في جواب ما هو تحسبا لشركة المحنة أو لا نكون وهو المقول يغ جوَابَ وَسُرُوكدً الى العام ما هو حسب الشركة والخصوصية معًا. اما حسب الخصوصية فظَاهِم نا ماعى الشركة فلان كل ما لكل واحد منها من الثانيات حاصل للآخر والا فضا ختص بعضها بما ليس الاخر فيكون الاختلاف بينه وبين ذلك الافو ليس العدد فقط بل بالثانيات وقد فرضنا انه لير كذلك هنا خلف ماذا كان تمام ما كل واحد منها من الذاتيات مشتركا بينه وبين غيره فلاحهم كان ذكره جواب عن السؤال عن ماهية تلك الانشاء حسب الشركة والخصوصية تاما الدال على جزء الماهية فقط ان يكون، والأعلى كمال الجزء الذى به يشارك عزه وهو الجنس وهو المقول في جواب ماه، حسب الشركة الحصة دانا ومغاير له اعتبادًا او كمال الجزء الذى به منان عن غيره وهو الفصل او لا نكون واحدًا من المنتمين وسبعين الدلالة الواضحة انشاء الله تعالى انه لابد وان يكون ذلك حنين خير او جنين فصل أو فصل حنين و فصل فقير على اخلاف مراتها
The quiddity of a single person is what is said in answer to 'what is it' with pure particularity, or the common quiddity of persons. And it is either differing in some essential, so the whole common part is said in answer to 'what is it' according to common participation; or it is not, and it is said in answer to 'what is it' going to the common according to participation and particularity together. As for particularity, it is apparent; as for commonality, because everything that each one has of accidents is attained by the other, otherwise some would be specified with what is not for the other, so the difference between it and that other is not only in number but in accidents; and we assumed it is not so, so there is a contradiction. If the whole of what each one has of essentials is common between it and others, then its mention is an answer to the question about the quiddity of those things according to commonality and particularity, fully indicative of only part of the quiddity. Or it is the completion of the part by which it participates with others, which is the genus, and it is said in answer to 'what is it' according to common participation, different from it in consideration; or the completion of the part by which it is distinguished from others, which is the differentia; or it is not one of the two, and the clear indication, if God wills, is that it must be either a genus and a differentia, or a differentia and a genus, or a differentia, or a genus, according to the variation of its degrees.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage defines two of the five universals: species (naw') and genus (jins), in relation to the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?). The author explains that when asked about the quiddity of a single person, the answer can be either particular (indicating the unique nature) or common (indicating the shared nature). If the answer includes only the common part, that part is a genus; if it includes the distinguishing part, it is a differentia. The answer must be either a genus, a differentia, or both. This is a foundational logical definition used in Islamic logic to structure definitions.

Key Terms
النوع
الجنس
الفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Species and genus are defined by how they answer 'ma huwa': species includes particularity and commonality, genus only commonality.

Hierarchy of Genera and Species and Nature of Differentia
Author explains hierarchy of genera and species and shares of differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relative nature of genus and species and explain how differentiae share a common genus.

Core Idea
Genus and species are relative; chain ends with highest genus and lowest species, differentiae share a common genus.

In Islamic logic, the five universals are not fixed; they exist in a hierarchy. This passage from Razi's Mulakhkhas explains how genera become species relative to higher genera, leading to a highest genus and a lowest species. It also clarifies that differentiae are not isolated but share a common genus with other differentiae. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for constructing valid definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the relativity of genus and species
The author states that a genus can include something that falls under it, making that something a species relative to the higher genus. Notice that 'genus' and 'species' are not fixed labels; they depend on the level of abstraction.
2
Identify the highest genus (jins al-ajnas)
The passage says genera ascend until a genus with no genus above it is reached. This is the most universal genus, called jins al-ajnas (genus of genera). Trace the chain upward in your mind.
3
Identify the lowest species (naw' al-anwa')
Similarly, species descend to a species with no species beneath it, called naw' al-anwa' (species of species). This is the most specific level.
4
Analyze the nature of differentia (al-fasl)
The author notes that differentiae share with other differentiae by falling under a common genus. This means a differentia is not a genus itself but is contained within a genus that includes multiple differentiating characteristics.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In usul al-fiqh, consider the definition of 'riba' (usury). Its genus is 'increase in financial transaction'. This genus is itself a species under 'financial transaction'. The differentia for riba (e.g., 'without compensation') shares the genus 'increase' with other differentiae like 'with compensation'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تم أن الجنس قد تشاول غير من الادراج تحت حراج فيكو نوعا بالنسبة اليه ثم ان الاجناس قد ترتب متصاعدة لا الى الانهاية لكن اليما لا جنس فوقه وهو جنس الاجناس الانواع قد يترتب حسنائلة الى ما لا نوع محته وهو نون الانواع والفصل قد شارك غيره الضام الاندراج على جليس الحق
Then the genus may include something that falls under a single category, so it becomes a species relative to it. Then genera ascend without end, but there is a genus above which there is no genus, which is the genus of genera. Species descend to what has no species beneath it, which is the species of species. And the differentia shares with other differentiae in falling under the same genus.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage discusses the hierarchical ordering of genera and species. Genera can be considered species relative to a higher genus, and this ascending chain continues until a highest genus (jins al-ajnas) is reached, which has no genus above it. Conversely, species descend to a lowest species (naw' al-anwa'), which has no species beneath it. The author also notes that differentiae share with other differentiae by being under the same genus, meaning that differentiae are not independent but are subsumed under a genus that contains multiple differentiae.

Key Terms
جنس الأجناس (jins al-ajnas): The highest genus, which has no genus above it; the most universal genus. [Note: Stable; used in all logical texts.]
نوع الأنواع (naw' al-anwa'): The lowest species, which has no species beneath it; the most specific species. [Note: Stable.]
الفصل (al-fasl): Differentia: a part of the quiddity that distinguishes the species, sharing with other differentiae under a common genus. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Genera and species are relative; the hierarchy culminates in a highest genus and a lowest species, and differentiae share a common genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Property and Common Accident: External Attributes in the Five Universals
Author defines property and common accident as external attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between property (khaassah) and common accident ('arad 'amm) and correctly classify any given external attribute as one of these two universals.

Core Idea
Property is unique to one species; common accident spans multiple species.

In the previous lesson, you learned the five universals. Now we delve into two that are external to the quiddity: property (al-khaassah) and common accident (al-'arad al-'amm). Understanding the distinction is crucial for rigorous definition in Islamic sciences such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify if attribute is internal or external
Determine whether the attribute is part of the essence (internal) or an accidental quality (external). Only external attributes can be property or common accident.
For example, 'rationality' (al-nutq) is internal to humans, while 'laughter' (al-dahak) is external.
2
Check distribution across species
If external, ask: Is this attribute found exclusively in one species, or does it appear in multiple species? Exclusive → property (khaassah). Shared → common accident ('arad 'amm).
The ability to laugh is exclusive to humans; blackness is shared by crows and humans.
3
Apply to Islamic science examples
Test your classification using examples from usul al-fiqh. For instance, intoxication is a common accident shared by wine and nabidh, allowing qiyas. A unique attribute of a specific animal might be a property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما حالا از کون لم وتم عام المالية والمعود مج كا لحدوث للحمر و الثاني كسماد النبي في الوصف الخارجي اما ان يعتبر من حيث انة مختص بنوع واحد لا يوجد في غيره وهو الخاصة أو من حيث انه موجود ة اكثر من نوع واحد وهو العرض العام
As for the external description, it is either considered in that it is specific to one species and is not found in other species, which is the property (khaassah); or in that it exists in more than one species, which is the common accident ('arad 'amm).
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines two of the five universals: property (khaassah) and common accident ('arad 'amm). Both are external attributes, meaning they are not part of the quiddity. Property is unique to a single species, e.g., the ability to laugh is unique to humans. Common accident is shared across multiple species, e.g., the color black may be found in both crows and humans. The distinction is based on exclusivity to one species versus occurrence in multiple species.

Key Terms
الخاصة
العرض العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Property is an external attribute unique to one species; common accident is an external attribute shared by multiple species.

Actually Hajj is only performed by humans, so it could be a property).

Four Types of Word-Meaning Relationship
Author classifies lafz based on multiplicity of word and meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given word-meaning pair into one of the four categories: mutawāṭi', ʿalam, mutarādifa, mushtaraka, manqūl, or mushakkik.

Core Idea
A word's relation to meaning is either one-to-one, one-to-many, many-to-one, or many-to-many.

In Islamic logic, precision begins with the word. Before we can define a term or construct an argument, we must understand whether a word has a single meaning or multiple, and whether that meaning applies to one thing or many. Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī lays out a simple but powerful classification of the four possible relationships between a word (lafẓ) and its meaning (maʿnā). This classification is the first step in avoiding ambiguity and mastering the tools of definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the multiplicity of the word and meaning
For a given word, determine if the word is singular or plural (i.e., one word or multiple words?), and if the meaning is singular or plural (i.e., one meaning or multiple meanings?). This gives four combinations.
2
Apply the first combination (word singular, meaning singular)
If both are singular, then check whether the concept can apply to many individuals. If yes, it is mutawāṭi' (univocal); if no, it is ʿalam (proper noun).
E.g., 'insān' applies to many humans, so mutawāṭi'; 'Zayd' applies to one person, so ʿalam.
3
Apply the second combination (word plural, meaning plural)
If both are plural, then the words are mutarādifa (synonyms): multiple words with the same meaning.
E.g., 'lays' and 'asad' both mean lion.
4
Apply the third combination (word singular, meaning plural)
If the word is singular but the meaning is plural, then it is mushtaraka (homonym): one word with multiple unrelated meanings.
E.g., 'ʿayn' can mean eye, spring, etc.
5
Apply the fourth combination (word singular, meaning plural, but with equality or gradation)
If the word is singular and meaning plural, further check: if the meanings are equal in applicability, it is manqūl (transferred); if graded, it is mushakkik (equivocal).
E.g., 'wujūd' is mushakkik when applied to God and creatures; 'ṣalāt' is manqūl from supplication to ritual prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأحوال اللفظ بالنسبة إلى المعنى على أربعة أوجه: إما أن يتحد اللفظ ويتحد المعنى، أو يكثر اللفظ ويكثر المعنى، أو يكثر اللفظ ويتحد المعنى، أو يتحد اللفظ ويكثر المعنى. فالأول: إن كان مفهومه ممكن الحصول في كثيرين فهو المتواطئ، وإلا فهو العلم. والثاني: الأسماء المترادفة. والثالث: الأسماء المشتركة. والرابع: إن كانت وضعه لها على التساوي فهو المنقول، وإن كان على التكاون فهو المشكك.
The states of the word with respect to meaning are of four types: either the word is singular and the meaning is singular, or the word is plural and the meaning is plural, or the word is plural and the meaning is singular, or the word is singular and the meaning is plural. The first: if its concept can obtain in many, it is the univocal (mutawāṭi'), otherwise it is the proper noun (ʿalam). The second: synonyms (mutarādifa). The third: homonyms (mushtaraka). The fourth: if its coinage is for equality, it is the transferred (manqūl), and if for gradation, it is the equivocal (mushakkik).
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage defines the four possible relationships between a word (laẓ) and its meaning (maʿnā) based on whether each is singular or plural. The author first lists the four combinations: (1) both singular, (2) both plural, (3) word plural meaning singular, (4) word singular meaning plural. For each, he gives a technical name. The first case (both singular) is further split: if the concept can apply to many individuals, it is mutawāṭi' (univocal), e.g., 'insān' applies to many humans; if it applies to only one, it is ʿalam (proper noun), e.g., the name 'Zayd'. The second case (both plural) yields synonyms (mutarādifa), different words with the same meaning, like 'lays' and 'asad' for lion. The third case (word plural, meaning singular) yields homonyms (mushtaraka), a single word with multiple meanings, e.g., 'ʿayn' for eye, spring, etc. The fourth case (word singular, meaning plural) yields either manqūl (transferred) if the meanings are equal in applicability, or mushakkik (equivocal) if they have gradation, e.g., 'wujūd' (existence) applied to God and creatures analogically. This classification is foundational for understanding ambiguity and precision in logical discourse.

Key Terms
متواطئ
علم
مترادفة
مشتركة
منقول
مشكك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which term describes a word that applies to many individuals equally, like 'insān' to all humans?
A) ʿalam
B) mushtarak
C) mutawāṭi'
D) manqūl

Key Takeaway
Al-Rāzī's fourfold classification of lafẓ-maʿnā relations provides the logical foundation for distinguishing univocal, proper nouns, synonyms, homonyms, transferred, and graded terms.

Three Types of Universal Singular Words: Ism al-Jins, 'Alam, Mushtaq
Author classifies singular words into proper nouns, genus nouns, and derived terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given singular Arabic word is an ism al-jins, 'alam, or mushtaq based on what it signifies, and explain the logical difference between them.

Core Idea
Singular words are universal if their meaning can apply to many; among them, three types: essence-noun, proper noun, and attribute-noun.

The logical study of words begins with the singular term. Before you can construct definitions or syllogisms, you must know what kind of meaning a word carries. In this passage, the author classifies singular words into universal and particular, and then divides universal words into three critical types: ism al-jins, 'alam, and mushtaq. Understanding this classification is the first step to precise reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Step 1: Determine universality
Check whether the word's meaning can apply to many individuals. If yes, it is universal (kullī); if it applies to only one, it is particular (juz'ī). For example, 'insān' applies to all humans, so it is universal; 'Zayd' applies only to one person, so it is particular.
This distinction is fundamental: only universal terms can be used as predicates in categorical propositions.
2
Step 2: Identify the type of universal
If the word is universal, look at what it signifies: does it signify an essence stripped of all specification (ism al-jins), a specific individual (ʿalam), or an attribute without specifying the essence of the subject (mushtaq)?
Ism al-jins: e.g., 'insān' (humanity). ʿAlam: e.g., 'Muḥammad'. Mushtaq: e.g., 'kātib' (writer).
3
Step 3: Test with examples
Take an Arabic word from an Islamic science text. Ask: (1) Is it universal? (2) If yes, does it signify an essence, a particular person/place, or a derived attribute? Classify it accordingly.
Practice with words like 'Allāh' (ʿalam), 'ḥayawān' (ism al-jins), 'ʿālim' (mushtaq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المفرد: فإما أن يكون موضوعًا لمعنى يمكن حصوله في كثيرين أولاً. فإن كان الأول فهو الكلي، وإن كان الثاني فهو الجزئي. والكلي: إما أن يكون موضوعًا لماهية معراة عن كل تعين فهو اسم الجنس، وإما أن يكون موضوعًا لشخص معين فهو العلم، وإما أن يكون موضوعًا لصفة معينة من غير دلالة على خصوصية ماهية الموصوف فهو المشتق.
As for the singular [word]: either it is coined for a meaning that can occur in many [individuals] or not. If the first, then it is universal (kullī); if the second, then it is particular (juz'ī). And the universal: either it is coined for an essence stripped of all specification, so it is a genus noun (ism al-jins); or it is coined for a specific individual, so it is a proper noun (ʿalam); or it is coined for a specific attribute without indication of the particularity of the essence of the described, so it is a derived term (mushtaq).
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of singular words (lafẓ mufrad) based on what they signify. First, the author distinguishes between universal (kullī) and particular (juz'ī) meanings: if the word's meaning can apply to many, it is universal; if only to one, it is particular. Then, within universal words, he identifies three categories. The first is ism al-jins (genus noun), which signifies an essence (māhiyya) devoid of any individuating restrictions, e.g., 'insān' (human) signifies the essence of humanity. The second is ʿalam (proper noun), which signifies a specific individual, e.g., 'Zayd'. The third is mushtaq (derived term), which signifies an attribute (ṣifa) without implying the specific essence of the subject, e.g., 'kātib' (writer) signifies the attribute of writing but does not specify whether the subject is a human or angel. This division is crucial for understanding how words relate to concepts and definitions.

Key Takeaway
Singular universal words fall into three categories: ism al-jins (essence), 'alam (specific individual), and mushtaq (attribute) — a distinction that underlies all logical definitions.

Six Categories of Universal Based on Existence
Author defines universal and lists its categories by existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal concept into one of the six existence categories and identify examples from Islamic theology and cosmology.

Core Idea
A universal is not defined by having many existents, but by its mental applicability to many.

In this passage, the author shows that universal concepts (kulliyyāt) are not limited to those with many individuals. Some have none, some have one, and some have many. Understanding these categories helps avoid confusion when discussing existence and conceptualization in logic and kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The author defines 'al-kullī' (the universal) by enumerating categories based on existence, not by a genus-differentia definition. Locate the term in the Arabic.
2
List the six categories
Go through each: (1) impossible existence, (2) possible but unknown, (3) unique and necessary, (4) unique but possible, (5) finite many, (6) infinite many. Note the Arabic phrases.
The order is from least to greatest in terms of actual instances.
3
Match each category to an example
Understand why each example fits: partner to God is impossible; phoenix is possible unknown; Allah is unique necessary; sun is unique possible; planets are finite many; humans are infinite many.
The sun example reflects pre-modern cosmology; the key is the conceptual category.
4
Apply the classification
Given a concept like 'angel' or 'jinn', determine which category it belongs to based on Islamic belief.
Consider whether the concept has any existing individuals and if more are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلى: قد يكون ممتنع الوجود كشريك الباري، وقد يكون ممكن الوجود لكن لا يعرف وجوده كعنقاء، وقد يكون موجودًا لكن منع أن يكون في الوجود منه أكثر من واحد كالله تعالى، وقد يكون الموجود منه واحد فقط وإن جاز وجود مثله كالشمس، وقد يكون الموجود أكثر من واحد بأشخاص كثيرة متناهية كالكواكب، وقد يكون غير متناهية كالإنسان. والجزئي يقال بالاشتراك على الشخص ومعنى آخر: والفرق أن الجزئي بالمعنى الأول عَرَض مضاف ولا كلي، وبالمعنى الثاني مضاف إلى ما فوقه.
The universal: it may be impossible of existence, like a partner to God; or it may be possible of existence but its existence is not known, like the phoenix; or it may exist but be prevented from having more than one in existence, like God the Almighty; or it may have only one existing [individual] but it is possible to have another like it, like the sun; or it may have more than one existing in many finite individuals, like the planets; or it may have infinite [individuals], like human. And the particular is said homonymously of the individual and another meaning. The difference: the particular in the first sense is an accidental relative and not universal; in the second sense it is relative to what is above it.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage classifies universal concepts (kulliyyāt) according to the actual existence of their individuals. The author enumerates six categories: (1) impossible: no individual can exist, e.g., a partner to God; (2) possible but unknown: the concept can exist but we have no evidence, e.g., the phoenix; (3) unique: only one individual exists and cannot have another, e.g., God; (4) unique but possible: only one exists but others are possible, e.g., the sun (in ancient cosmology); (5) finite many: multiple individuals but limited, e.g., planets; (6) infinite: unlimited individuals, e.g., humans. Then the author clarifies that the term 'juz'ī' (particular) is ambiguous: it can mean the concrete individual (a non-universal by itself), or it can mean a concept subordinate to a higher universal (relative particularity). This distinction is important to avoid confusion when discussing generality and specificity.

Key Takeaway
The universal is defined by its conceptual applicability to many, not by the actual multiplicity of its referents in the external world.

Two Senses of 'Particular': Individual and Subordinate Concept
Author clarifies two meanings of juz'ī (particular).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of 'juz'ī' and apply the correct sense in logical analysis.

Core Idea
Juz'ī can mean either a concrete individual or a concept subordinate to a broader universal — confusing them leads to logical errors.

The author clarifies that the term 'juz'ī' is used homonymously: it can refer to a specific individual (like Zayd) or to a concept that is more specific than another (like 'human' relative to 'animal'). Understanding this prevents ambiguity in discussions about generality and specificity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings
Find the phrase 'يقال بالاشتراك على الشخص ومعنى آخر'. The first meaning is the concrete individual (al-shakhs). The second meaning is a concept subordinate to a higher universal (mudāf ilā mā fawqah).
2
Understand the first meaning
In the first sense, juz'ī is an accidental relative (ʿaraḍ muḍāf) and is not universal at all. It refers to a particular entity like Zayd.
This sense is absolute particularity.
3
Understand the second meaning
In the second sense, juz'ī is relative to what is above it. For example, 'human' is juz'ī relative to 'animal', but is kullī relative to 'Zayd'.
This sense is relational; the same concept can be both universal and particular from different perspectives.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a term like 'عالم' (scholar), determine which sense is intended based on context. For example, in 'كل عالم فاضل', it is universal; in 'العالم زيد', it is particular in the first sense.
Context clues: if the term is predicated of many, it is likely universal; if it refers to a named individual, it is particular in the first sense.

The Three Types of Universal
Author defines the three types of universal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the natural, logical, and mental universal in any definitional context.

Core Idea
The universal exists in three modes: in external reality, as a predicate, and as a mental image.

This passage from al-Razi’s Mulakhkhas introduces a crucial tripartite division of the universal (kulli). You will learn how the same nature can be considered as existing in individuals, as a logical relation, and as a mental representation. This distinction underpins definitions (hadd) and prevents confusion between ontology and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types named
Find the three phrases: الكلي الطبيعي, الكلي المنطقي, الكلي العقلي. Each corresponds to a different perspective on universality.
They are listed in order: natural, logical, mental.
2
Understand the natural universal
The natural universal is the common nature (e.g., animality) as it exists in individual beings. It is not a separate entity but the shared essence.
Al-Razi says it has no separate existence outside the individuals (فلسكري وجود مع الأعيان).
3
Understand the logical universal
The logical universal is the universal considered as a predicate that can be said of many. It falls under the category of relation (المضاف) because its meaning depends on its applicability to multiple subjects.
Al-Razi notes it is a type of relation (نوع من مقولة المضاف).
4
Understand the mental universal
The mental universal is the mental image (الصورة الذهنية) in the intellect that is described as universal. Although it is a particular image, it represents many individuals.
The text argues it must exist because pure non-existence cannot be shared.
5
Apply the distinction to a definition
Given a definition like 'الإنسان حيوان ناطق', identify which type of universal each term is: 'حيوان' is the natural universal (the genus), 'ناطق' is the differentia, but the definition as a whole is a logical universal because it predicates something of many.
Practice with other definitions from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في البنية إلى الطبيعة طبعي كما قالوا ني حنيفة حنفي إلا أن الحكماء جودوا على الأصل فلم عند قول البخاري كما أنشده محمد بن حمديان. يعرض للحيوان بالنسبة إلى موضوعاته والنسبة بين السيسين متأخرة عنها فالحلبة متأخرة عن ماهية الحيوان وماهية الإنسان متأخرة عن ماهية الحيوان لوجوب تأخر المركب عن مفرداته. إذا عرفت ذلك ظهر أن المركب عنها مغاير لها فلكل واحد منهما اسم خاص، والأول هو الكلي الطبيعي والثاني الكلي المنطقي والثالث الكلي العقلي. أما الكلي الطبيعي فلسكري وجود مع الأعيان لأن الحيوان جوه بين هذا الحيوان ومن كان المركب موجودا كان البسيط موجودا والحال أن الموجود يتركب عن المعدوم. وأما الكلي المنطقي فهو نوع من مقولة المضاف وسيأتي العلامة أن الإضافة هل لها وجود في الأعيان أم لا. وأما الكلي العقلي فالمشهور أنه هو الصورة الذهنية في الآلة الموصوف بالكلية موجود لأن العدم الصرف تتخيل أن يكون مشتركا فيه بين كثيرين وكل موجود فإما في الخارج أو في الذهن والأول محال لأن كل موجود في الخارج في شخص معين متميز عن كل ما عداه وكل ما كان كذلك لم يكن مشتركا فيه بين كثيرين فلم يكن كليا.
In terms of relationship to nature, it is natural as they said ... Abu Hanifa Hanafi, except that the philosophers adhered to the original. So there is no [valid] according to the saying of al-Bukhari as recited by Muhammad ibn Hamdan. [The universal] occurs to [the concept of] animal relative to its subjects, and the relation between the two is posterior to them, so the truth is posterior to the quiddity of animal, and the quiddity of man is posterior to the quiddity of animal due to the necessity of the composite being posterior to its parts. Once you know that, it becomes clear that the composite is other than its parts, so each of the two has a specific name: the first is the natural universal, the second is the logical universal, and the third is the mental universal. As for the natural universal, it has no existence with the individuals, because animal is a substance between this animal and ... If the composite exists, the simple exists, but it is impossible for the existent to be composed from the non-existent. As for the logical universal, it is a type of the category of relation, and the sign will come whether relation has existence in individuals or not. As for the mental universal, the well-known [view] is that it is the mental image in the instrument described as universality, which exists because pure non-existence cannot be imagined to be common among many. Everything that exists is either in the external world or in the mind, the first is impossible because everything that exists externally is a specific individual distinguished from everything else, and everything that is like that cannot be common among many, so it is not universal.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of universal (kulli) according to the logicians. The author begins with a brief remark about natural vs. something else, then explains that a composite (like a definition) is posterior to its parts. He then identifies three types: the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i), the logical universal (al-kulli al-mantiqi), and the mental universal (al-kulli al-'aqli). The natural universal is the nature considered in itself, existing in the external individuals; it is not itself a separate existent but the common nature shared by individuals. The logical universal is the universal as a predicate, which falls under the category of relation (mudaf) because it is relative to the many individuals it can be predicated of. The mental universal is the mental image (sura dhihniyya) in the mind that is described as universal. The author then argues that this mental image must exist because pure non-existence cannot be shared; yet it cannot exist externally because external things are particular. Thus its existence is mental. This sets up the problem of how a particular mental image can represent a universal nature, which will be addressed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
الكلي المنطقي
الكلي العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Natural universal
B) Logical universal
C) Mental universal
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The universal can be considered in three distinct ways: as a nature in external individuals (natural), as a predicate relative to many (logical), and as a mental image representing that nature (mental).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Razi's Critique of the Mental Universal
Al-Razi deconstructs the logicians' answer on the mental universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's key objection to the logicians' account of the mental universal and explain why he considers the mental universal only metaphorical.

Core Idea
The mental image is particular; the true universal is the external common nature.

The logicians faced a puzzle: how can a mental image, which is a particular entity in the mind, be universal? They gave an answer involving abstraction. Al-Razi finds this answer deeply problematic. In this lesson, you will dissect his critique and see why he rejects the mental universal as merely metaphorical, setting the stage for his own realist alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' problem and answer
Read the opening: they ask how a mental image can be universal, and answer by explaining the process of abstraction. This is the target of the critique. Notice they admit the mental image is particular (شخصية).
The logicians' answer tries to explain universality without the mental image itself being shared.
2
Find al-Razi's first objection
He says 'هذا بناء على الصورة الذهنية وعندنا باطلة' – the entire theory of mental image is false in his view. This is a radical rejection from his broader philosophical system.
This objection is ontological: mental images as real entities do not exist.
3
Find the second objection
Even if mental images existed, they cannot explain universality because the image itself is particular. The true universal is the shared nature in external things. Al-Razi argues the mental image is only metaphorically universal.
The mental image corresponds to the common nature but is not itself universal.
4
Conclude: rejection of the standard division
The division of the universal into three types (based on mental image) is wrong because it relies on a false foundation. The true universal is the external common nature.
This sets up his alternative view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سألوا أنفسهم وقالوا: الصورة الذهنية صورة شخصية لها أحوال تخصها فكيف تكون كلية؟ فأجابوا: إذا قلنا للصورة الذهنية إنها كلية فلا نعني بها كونها بعينها مشتركا فيها بين الأشخاص الخارجية بل المراد أن أي واحد من الأشخاص الموجودة في الخارج من النوع الواحد إذا سبق إلى الذهن بدلا عن غيره، وأخذت النفس ما يساوي تلك الماهية مجردة عن خصوصياتها، لم يكن الحاصل في النفس إلا ذلك الأثر. هذا انتهى كلامهم وهو مشكل من وجوه: هذا بناء على الصورة الذهنية وعندنا باطلة، وبتقدير القول بها لكنه لا يصح تفسير الكلية بها، وكان تقسيم الكلية إلى الثلاثة المفسر عجز الماهية خطأ، لأن الصورة الذهنية التي قد تكون حادثة بعد هذا ممنوع. حدوث الشخص بمقتضى أن تكون جزءا من ماهية الشخص الموجود في الخارج، المراد من قولنا إن انتزعت النفس عن كل ملك الأشخاص واحد معنا نتصور قدر مشترك ملك الأشخاص تصور القدر المشترك إن لم يتوقف على تحقق القدر المشترك لم تكن الصورة الذهنية مطابقة للأمر الخارجي وبالجملة، وإن كان مطابقا فلا بد من حصول قدر مشترك في نفس الأمر، فذلك الأمر المشترك هو الكلي في الحقيقة، والصورة الذهنية إنما تسمى كلية مجازا لكونها علما متعلقا بما هو الأمر الكلي. والذي نقوله: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن أشخاص النوع الواحد مشتركة في طبيعة ذلك النوع، وأن كل واحد منها يمتاز عما عداه بخصوصية، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز، فذلك الأمر المشترك هو الكلي، فكون الكلي موجودا في الخارج. وأما الذي يعتقده المشهور من ثبات متحدة مجردة في الذهن فسنبطلها في الحكمة إن شاء الله تعالى. والكلي فانه أعرف من الجزئي لكن عدمه أخصر من عدم الجزئي، فإنه لما كان كلما صدق عليه اللون صدق عليه السواد وانعكس حكمنا بأن اللون أعم من السواد، ولكنه ليس كلما صدق اللون صدق اللا سواد ولا ينعكس، فيكون اللون أعم من اللا سواد. ولهيت أن الذي يغاير هذا الشخص أكثر مما يغاير هذا النوع، كل معقولين فلا بد وأن يكون أحد ما مع الآخر إلا أخصر من مطلقا أو أعم منه مطلقا أو لا أعم ولا أخصر أو أعم من وجه وأخصر من وجه كالحيوان والأبيض وذلك ممكن. فأما أن يكون أعم منه مطلقا أو أخص مطلقا أو من وجه، فأما مساو فلا. الكلي أعرف من الجزئي للاستقراء أو الحجة، فإن الأعم أقل شرطا ومعاندا من الأخص، وما كان كذلك كان أكثر وقوعا في الذهن وأعرف. إدراك الشخص الواحد المعين من حيث إنه ذلك الشخص إما بالوجدان وهو ما يعا كل أحد ذاته المعينة من حيث هي هي، أو بالخيرة، مثل إذا أشرنا إلى هذا الشخص المشار إليه. وههنا بحث وسوان الخير يعلق به من حيث هو هو.
Then they asked themselves and said: The mental image is a personal image that has its own specific circumstances, so how can it be universal? So they answered: When we say of the mental image that it is universal, we do not mean that it itself is shared among external individuals, but rather that any one of the external individuals of the same type that comes to the mind instead of another, and the soul abstracts what is equivalent to that quiddity stripped of its particularities, then what is obtained in the soul is nothing but that effect. This ends their statement, and it is problematic in several ways: This is based on the mental image, which in our view is false; and even if it were accepted, it is not valid to interpret universality by it, and the division of the universal into the three [types] that explain the deficiency of quiddity is wrong, because the mental image that may come into being after this is prevented. The occurrence of the individual by virtue of being part of the quiddity of the individual existing externally: the meaning of our saying 'the soul abstracts from all the individuals of one kind' is that we conceive a common denominator among the individuals. If the conception of the common denominator does not depend on the existence of the common denominator, then the mental image would not correspond to the external thing; in short, if it does correspond, then there must be a common denominator in reality, and that common thing is the true universal, and the mental image is only called universal metaphorically because it is a knowledge related to what is the universal thing. And what we say: We know by necessity that the individuals of one type share the nature of that type, and that each one is distinguished from others by a particularity, and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished, so that shared thing is the universal, so the universal exists externally. As for what the common people believe about fixed, separate entities in the mind, we will refute it in philosophy, God willing. And the universal is more known than the particular, but its non-existence is more specific than the non-existence of the particular. For since whenever color is true, black is true, and the converse, we judge that color is more general than black; but it is not the case that whenever color is true, non-black is true, nor the converse, so color is more general than non-black. And the proof is that what differentiates this individual is more than what differentiates this type. Every two intelligibles must be either one is more specific absolutely, or more general absolutely, or not more general nor more specific, or more general in one way and more specific in another, like animal and white, and that is possible. So it is either more general absolutely, or more specific absolutely, or from one aspect, but equal is not. The universal is more known than the particular by induction or proof; for the more general has fewer conditions and oppositions than the more specific, and what is like that occurs more often in the mind and is more known. The perception of a specific individual in terms of its being that individual is either by inner sense (wijdan), which is what each one perceives of their own specific self as it is, or by pointing, such as when we point to this person indicated. And here is a discussion, and the goodness of pointing is attached to it in terms of its being what it is.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the mental universal. The logicians raise an objection: the mental image is a particular image in the mind, so how can it function as a universal? They answer by explaining that when the mind abstracts a quiddity from an external individual, it forms a mental concept that is applicable to any individual of that type, and that concept is called universal not because the mental image itself is shared, but because it corresponds to a common nature. The author (al-Razi) criticizes this answer from several angles. First, the theory of mental image itself is false in his view. Even if granted, it fails to explain universality because the mental image is a particular attribute of the mind and cannot be a true universal. The proper division of the universal is not into these three, because the mental universal is only metaphorically universal. The author then presents his own position: we necessarily know that individuals of the same type share a real common nature that exists externally. This common nature is the true universal. The mental image represents it but is not itself the universal. He further argues that the universal is more known than the particular, using an analogy with color and black: the more general has fewer conditions and is thus more easily grasped by the mind. He distinguishes two ways of knowing a particular: by inner awareness (wijdan) or by external indication (ishara). This passage is critical of the standard logical view and offers a realist alternative grounded in external existence.

Key Terms
الصورة الذهنية
الكلي الحقيقي
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is the mental image not a true universal?
A) Because it is a particular image in the mind
B) Because it is not shared by multiple individuals
C) Because it cannot be predicated
D) Because it is caused by external objects

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi rejects the mental image as a true universal, insisting that universality belongs to the common nature existing in external things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Summarize al-Razi's two main objections to the logicians' answer about the mental universal, using only the passage.
?
2. Explain why al-Razi says the mental image is only called universal 'metaphorically' (مجازا). What is the real universal according to him?

The Five Universals
advanced
8 min
Al-Razi's Alternative: The External Universal and Its Knowability
Al-Razi's alternative account of the universal and its epistemic priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Razi's positive account of the universal as an external common nature and his argument that the universal is more known than the particular.

Core Idea
The universal is an external reality, more known than the particular due to fewer conditions.

After criticizing the mental universal, al-Razi presents his own view: the true universal is the common nature existing externally in individuals. He then argues that the universal is more known than the particular, using an analogy with color and black. This lesson covers his alternative and the epistemological argument that grounds our knowledge of universals as immediate and necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Razi's positive claim
He asserts that we know by necessity that individuals of a type share a common nature (طبيعة ذلك النوع). That nature is distinct from individuating particularities.
This is presented as self-evident (ضروري).
2
Understand the external existence of the universal
Al-Razi says 'فذلك الأمر المشترك هو الكلي' and 'فكون الكلي موجودا في الخارج' – the shared thing is the universal, and it exists in external individuals, not just in the mind.
This contrasts with the mental universal of the logicians.
3
Analyze the argument that the universal is more known
He uses the example of color and black: color is more general and has fewer conditions, so it is more easily known. The same applies to universals: what has fewer conditions occurs more in the mind and is better known.
The principle: the more general is epistemically prior.
4
Distinguish two ways of knowing particulars
Particulars are known either by inner sense (wijdan) – e.g., one's own self – or by pointing (ishara) – e.g., pointing to an external object. This explains how we grasp individuals.
This complements the epistemic priority of universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

واحد من الأشخاص الموجودة في الخارج من النوع الواحد إذا سبق إلى الذهن بدلا عن غيره، وأخذت النفس ما يساوي تلك الماهية مجردة عن خصوصياتها، لم يكن الحاصل في النفس إلا ذلك الأثر. هذا انتهى كلامهم وهو مشكل من وجوه: هذا بناء على الصورة الذهنية وعندنا باطلة، وبتقدير القول بها لكنه لا يصح تفسير الكلية بها، وكان تقسيم الكلية إلى الثلاثة المفسر عجز الماهية خطأ، لأن الصورة الذهنية التي قد تكون حادثة بعد هذا Razi's Argument for the Reality of Universals
Author defends realism of universals against the common view that they are merely mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Razi's argument for the external existence of universals and evaluate its logical structure.

Core Idea
Universals are not just mental labels; they exist in external individuals as shared natures.

The question of whether universals (the common nature shared by individuals of a species) exist in the external world or only in the mind is a classic problem in Islamic philosophy. In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a powerful argument for realism. Let's trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify Razi's starting premise
Razi begins with a conditional: if a mental image (like the concept of 'human') were independent of any common reality, it would not correspond to external things. Why does he think that? Because a mental concept that applies to multiple individuals must reflect something real they share.
Consider: if 'human' is just a name, why would it apply equally to Zayd and Amr?
2
Add the observed fact of correspondence
Razi states that our mental images do correspond to external objects: the concept 'human' accurately describes Zayd and Amr. So the antecedent of the conditional (that the image does not depend on common reality) must be false.
This is a modus tollens argument in disguise.
3
Conclude the existence of common reality
Therefore, there must be a common reality (قدر مشترك) in the external world that the mental image corresponds to. This common reality is the universal (الكلي) as it exists in extra-mental individuals.
Razi calls this the 'true reality in truth'.
4
Support with the intuitive argument from species
Razi adds a second argument: we know by necessity that individuals of a species share a nature and each has an individuating property (شخصية). What is shared is distinct from what distinguishes; the shared aspect is the universal, proving its external existence.
This is an appeal to self-evident knowledge (بديهي) about the structure of species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنْ كَانَتِ الصُّورَةُ الذِّهْنِيَّةُ لَا تَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى تَحَقُّقِ الْقَدْرِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ لَمْ تَكُنْ مُطَابِقَةً لِلْأَمْرِ الْخَارِجِيِّ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُطَابِقَةً فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ حُصُولِ قَدْرٍ مُشْتَرَكٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، فَذَلِكَ الْأَمْرُ الْمُشْتَرَكُ هُوَ الْحَقِيقِيُّ فِي الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَالصُّورَةُ الذِّهْنِيَّةُ إِنَّمَا تُسَمَّى كُلِّيَّةً لِكَوْنِهَا عِلْمًا مُتَعَلِّقًا بِمَا هُوَ الْأَمْرُ الْكُلِّيُّ. وَالَّذِي تَقُولُهُ: إِنَّا نَعْلَمُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ أَنَّ أَشْخَاصَ النَّوْعِ الْوَاحِدِ مُشْتَرِكَةٌ فِي طَبِيعَةِ ذَلِكَ النَّوْعِ، وَأَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهَا يَتَمَيَّزُ عَمَّا عَدَاهُ بِشَخْصِيَّةٍ، وَمَا بِهِ الِاشْتِرَاكُ غَيْرُ مَا بِهِ الِامْتِيَازُ، فَذَلِكَ الْأَمْرُ الْمُشْتَرَكُ هُوَ الْكُلِّيُّ، فَكَوْنُ الْكُلِّيِّ مَوْجُودًا فِي الْخَارِجِ. وَأَمَّا الَّذِي يَعْتَقِدَهُ الْمَشْهُورُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِهَا مُجَرَّدَةً فِي الذِّهْنِ فَسَيَأْتِي إِبْطَالُهَا فِي الْحِكْمَةِ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى.
If the mental image does not depend on the realization of the common reality, it would not correspond to the external thing; and if it corresponds, then there must be a common reality in the mind-independent world. So that common thing is the true reality in truth, and the mental image is called universal only because it is knowledge related to what is the universal thing. And what you say: we know by necessity that the individuals of one species share in the nature of that species, and that each one of them is distinguished from others by an individuating property, and what is shared is other than what distinguishes; so that shared thing is the universal, and thus the universal exists externally. As for what the common people believe—that universals subsist as abstracts in the mind—its refutation will come in metaphysics, God willing.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a foundational argument for realism about universals. Razi begins with a conditional: if a mental image (like the concept of 'human') were independent of any common reality, it could not correspond to external individuals; but we do see correspondence (e.g., the concept 'human' applies to many individuals), so there must be a common nature (قدر مشترك) in reality. This common nature is what is truly real ('الحقيقي في الحقيقة'), and the mental concept is called 'universal' only because it is knowledge of that real universal. Razi then reinforces this with a direct intuitive argument: we know necessarily that individuals of a species share a nature and are distinguished by something else; the shared aspect is the universal, proving its external existence. He ends by noting that the opposing view (nominalism or conceptualism) will be refuted later in metaphysics. All examples in this commentary must draw from Islamic sciences: for instance, the fact that 'all prophets are human' and 'each prophet is distinct' shows a shared nature (humanity) existing in external individuals, not merely in the mind.

Key Terms
قدر مشترك
كلي
شخصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because mental images are always accurate.
B) Because if a mental image corresponds to multiple external objects, those objects must share a common nature.
C) Because the common people believe in universals.
D) Because metaphysics proves it.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that universals are real through a conditional argument from correspondence and an appeal to the self-evident structure of species.

Defining the Essential: A Logical Parsing of Al-Dhati
Author lists and critiques definitions of 'essential' (al-dhati) in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the essential (al-dhati) from accidental attributes by applying the verifiers' definition of 'part of the essence'.

Core Idea
The essential is not just what is inseparable, but what constitutes the essence itself.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents multiple definitions of 'essential' (al-dhati) from different logicians, each with a different scope. He then critiques these definitions, arguing that the real dispute is merely verbal, and the correct meaning according to the verifiers is that which is part of the essence (juz' al-mahiyya). Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing proper definitions and avoiding confusion between essential and accidental attributes in Islamic sciences such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main definitions listed
Scan the passage and list the four definitions: (1) that which the mind cannot conceive absence from, (2) that which the concept cannot be free of, (3) that which cannot be absent from the essence, (4) the specific definition (garbled) and finally the verifiers' definition: part of the essence.
The first three are broader and include necessary concomitants.
2
Analyze why the first definition is too broad
The first definition includes necessary concomitants like 'able to laugh' for humans, which are not constitutive parts of the essence. They are inseparable but not essential in the strict sense.
This shows that inseparability is not sufficient for essentiality.
3
Understand the verifiers' narrowing
The verifiers restrict the definition to 'part of the essence' (juz' al-mahiyya), so only genus and differentia qualify as essential. This excludes all accidental attributes, even if inseparable.
This aligns with the standard hylomorphic analysis.
4
Recognize the dispute as verbal
The author argues that the word 'essential' can be applied to many concepts; the real task is to distinguish the concepts themselves to avoid error in reasoning.
Focus on conceptual analysis rather than terminological squabbles.

Source Text

And they differed in the interpretation of the essential (al-dhati). Some of them interpreted it as that which prevents the mind from conceiving the absence of the thing, and all necessary concomitants of existence and essence are included in it. Some of them interpreted it more specifically than that, and it is that which prevents the concept of the thing in its essence from being free of it. Some of them interpreted it more specific than that, and it is that which prevents the absence of the thing from the essence. Some of them made it the specific, and it is what is between ... and the basic disjunction ... is what the love of preventing the awareness of the mind with its awareness of that description. And I said that the third is acceptable, because it is possible that the thing is such that ignorance of it occurs along with awareness of the subject, but it is from the domain of awareness, so the mind cannot be absent from it. And some of them made it more specific, and it is that which is a part of the essence, and they are the verifiers. And the dispute over these interpretations is verbal and not suitable for real investigations, and it is also manifold because the essential is one in language attributed to the essence without specification of being inside or outside, necessary or separable, and the root meaning is impossible because whoever interprets a word cannot be told that he is correct or wrong. Indeed, what is investigated is the effort to distinguish these concepts from each other, and to take from them the essence and the accidental attributes that occur to it, and the difference between them is more important, and to guard against error resulting from ambiguity. The part of the essence does not accept intensity and weakness because if the essence remains after the weakness of that part, that means the increase is redundant, and if it does not remain, the weakness is non-existence. And similarly, in the case of increase, if the essence remains, the increase is superfluous, and if not, that is all possible.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'essential' (al-dhati) in logic, presenting several interpretations and critiquing them. The author first lists different definitions: (1) that which the mind cannot conceive the absence of the thing from—this is too broad as it includes all necessary concomitants. (2) a more specific definition: that which the concept of the thing cannot be free of in its essence. (3) an even more specific one: that which cannot be absent from the essence. (4) some define it as the specific, which is described obscurely in the text. The author notes that the third definition is acceptable because it is possible to be unaware of some essential property while still being aware of the subject, but awareness of the subject implies awareness of its essential parts? The text becomes garbled. Finally, the preferred definition is that the essential is that which is a part of the essence (juz' al-mahiyya), and this is the view of the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun). The author then argues that the dispute over these definitions is merely verbal (lafzi) and not substantive, because 'essential' is a single linguistic term that can be applied to different concepts. The real task is to distinguish the concepts and avoid errors from ambiguity. The passage also discusses that a part of the essence cannot vary in intensity or weakness, using examples of essence remaining or not after modification.

Six Types of Attributes (Lawāḥiq)
Author enumerates six types of attributes based on essence/accident and breadth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any attribute into one of six types based on its attachment (by essence or by accident) and its breadth relative to the subject.

Core Idea
Attributes are six: three by essence, three by accident, each broader, equal, or narrower.

This passage from Rāzī's Mulakhkhaṣ organizes all possible attributes that attach to a thing into six clear types. The classification uses two criteria: how the attribute attaches (by the thing's own essence or via another attribute) and its breadth (broader, equal, or narrower) compared to the thing. Mastering this sixfold division is essential for constructing precise definitions and avoiding logical errors in demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main criteria
First, note that the passage divides attributes by 'by essence' (bi-al-dhāt) vs. 'by accident' (bi-al-'araḍ). Second, it divides them by breadth: broader (a'amm), equal (musāwī), or narrower (akhass).
These two criteria are independent, giving 2 × 3 = 6 types.
2
Understand 'by essence' vs 'by accident'
An attribute attaches 'by essence' if it belongs to the thing due to its own nature (e.g., 'animal' for man). It attaches 'by accident' if it belongs via another attribute (e.g., 'white' for man via the surface).
In logic, essential attributes are those that enter the definition; accidental ones do not.
3
Understand the three breadth relations
Broader (a'amm): the attribute applies to more individuals than the subject (e.g., 'animal' is broader than 'man'). Equal (musāwī): the attribute and subject apply to the exact same set (e.g., 'possible' and 'contingent'). Narrower (akhass): the attribute applies to fewer individuals (e.g., 'skilled in medicine' is narrower than 'man').
Breadth is always relative to the subject (the thing being described).
4
Combine dimensions to list all six types
Take each attachment mode and pair it with each breadth: (1) essential broader, (2) essential equal, (3) essential narrower, (4) accidental broader, (5) accidental equal, (6) accidental narrower. Memorize this grid.
The passage gives examples for each type, but the OCR is corrupted. The commentary suggests: animal (1), possible/contingent (2), laughing (3), etc.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Take a subject like 'human' and find attributes for each type: e.g., 'substance' is broader and essential; 'rational' is narrower and essential; 'risible' is equal and accidental; 'white' is broader and accidental; 'writer' is narrower and accidental. This solidifies the classification.
Ensure your examples match the type: check both attachment and breadth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And upon the description that attaches to the essence... like our saying: the animal is moving or more specific... either the attachment is for itself or for another, and in both cases that attachment may be broader, narrower, or equal. So these are six divisions. That which attaches by itself and is broader by mediation of man... like individuality for the soul? That which attaches by itself and is equal like our saying: the possible is in need... either by itself like finding power... and that which attaches by itself and is broader when it resolves into two things not specifically and each is more specific like existing with one and many? The other that attaches the description its equal... and from the attachments that attach to description its container? And it is the taking of bodies by mediation of its container? Not the solution of the predicate on man by mediation of al-Tahajji? Commentary
This passage classifies attributes (lawāḥiq) that attach to a thing into six types based on two criteria: whether the attribute attaches by essence or by something else, and whether it is broader (a'amm), equal (musāwī), or narrower (akhass) than the thing. The six types are: (1) by essence and broader, such as 'animal' for 'man' (animal is broader); (2) by essence and equal, such as 'possible' for 'contingent' (both are coextensive); (3) by essence and narrower, such as 'laughing' for 'man' (only some men laugh? Actually, laughter is specific to man by essence? The text may be garbled); (4) by another and broader; (5) by another and equal; (6) by another and narrower. Examples are given but the OCR corruption makes them unclear. This division is important for understanding definitions and properties in demonstration, as used in the Burhān (Posterior Analytics).

Does a Simple Quiddity Have Concomitants? Division of Concomitants
Author discusses whether a simple quiddity can have a concomitant and classifies concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the three types of concomitants (essential, accidental, separate) and evaluate the argument regarding a simple quiddity's ability to have multiple concomitants.

Core Idea
A simple quiddity can have multiple concomitants because concomitance can be essential or accidental.

In this lesson, we explore a key logical question: can a simple quiddity, which is not composed of parts, have necessary concomitants? The author challenges the philosophical principle that from one simple only one thing can proceed. He then provides a classification of concomitants that shows how a simple can be associated with multiple things, which is essential for understanding definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm) in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage opens by asking whether a simple quiddity can have a necessary concomitant. What is at stake? If a simple cannot have multiple concomitants, then defining something by its necessary properties would be impossible.
2
Understand the objection and refutation
An objection: if the quiddity necessitates the concomitant, the simple would be both receptive and active; if not, it has no concomitant. The author declares the premise 'from one only one' as false. Note that he rejects the emanationist principle.
This refutation is crucial for Islamic theology (Kalam) where God is simple yet has multiple attributes.
3
Recognize the threefold classification
The author divides concomitants into: essential (like accident necessarily accompanies substance), accidental (where the subject is essential but the concomitant follows from it not by essence), and separate (like the world's existence according to those who hold creation by choice).
Essential concomitants are necessary; accidental are contingent but necessarily follow from the subject's existence.
4
Compare two-sided vs one-sided concomitance
Two-sided: both are causally related (one cause or both effects of one cause). One-sided: the concomitant is from the essence itself. This refines the notion of 'luzum'.
One-sided concomitance applies to essential concomitants like accident-substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام فيه مستقصى المحكمة: أن الماهية البسيطة هل لها لازم أم لا؟ لمن أفكره أن نقول بتلك الماهية: إما أن تكون مقتضية وجود ذلك اللازم فحينئذ يكون البسيط قابلا وفاعلا معا، أو لا تقتضيه فلا يكون لها لازم، لأن البسيط لا يصدر عنه أكثر من واحد وهذه المقدمة عندنا باطلة. ثم إن سلمناها لكن لم الجون أن يكون اللازم من جانبين: أحدهما لذاتية والآخر لحوم الفيض؟ في تقسيم اللوازم سوى أمر وذلك: أن اللازم قد يكون لزومه ذاتيا كالعرض فإنه لذاته لازم الجوهر، وقد يكون الملزوم هو الذاتي واللازم عرضيا كوجود اللازم عن الملزوم، وقد يكون الأمر منفصلا كالوجود للعالم عند من يرى وجوب تأثير المؤثر بالاختيار. إما أن يكون اللزوم من الجانبين إما لذاتها لمكان المضافين أو لا بد حينئذ من أن يكون أحدهما علة للآخر أو كونا معلولي علة واحدة، فإنه لو لم تكن كذلك لكان كل واحد منها غنيا عن الآخر عن كل ما احتاج إليه الآخر وذلك يقتضي أن كل واحد منه دون الآخر وهو يقدح في اللزوم. ومن منع أن يصدر من البسيط أكثر من واحد يجوز في السلام أن يكون ذلك لكونها معلولي علة بل أوج أن يكون واحدا لأحد ما تقدم على الآخر. وإما أن يكون اللزوم من جانب واحد وذلك إذا كان اللازم من لوازم الذات
And the discussion therein is thorough: does the simple quiddity have a necessary concomitant or not? For one might consider that we say about that quiddity: either it necessitates the existence of that concomitant, then the simple would be both receptive and agent together, or it does not necessitate it, so it has no concomitant, because from a simple only one thing proceeds—and this premise is false according to us. Then if we accept it, why would it not be allowed that the concomitant be from two sides: one essential and the other from the flow [of emanation]? In the division of concomitants, there is an issue: the concomitant may be essential, like an accident that necessarily accompanies a substance; or the subject may be essential and the concomitant accidental, like the existence of the concomitant from the subject; or it may be separate, like existence for the world according to those who hold the necessity of the agent's effect by choice. Either the concomitance is from both sides—either because of the relational nature or because one must be a cause of the other or both effects of one cause, for if it were not so, each would be independent of the other, contradicting the concomitance. Whoever denies that from a simple more than one proceeds may allow in the [case of] peace that it be because they are effects of one cause, rather they must be one because one precedes the other. Or the concomitance may be from one side, namely when the concomitant is among the essential concomitants.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage examines the question of whether a simple quiddity (mahiyya basita) can have a necessary concomitant. The author presents a possible objection: if the quiddity is simple, it cannot be both receptive and active; if it does not necessitate the concomitant, then it has none, because from one simple only one thing can proceed. The author declares this premise false. He then discusses the division of concomitants: some are essential (like accidents necessarily belonging to a substance), others are accidental (where the concomitant follows from the subject but not by its essence), and others are separate (like the existence of the world according to those who hold creation by choice). He then analyzes two-sided concomitance, where each may be cause or both effects, and one-sided concomitance for essential concomitants. This refines the concept of 'lazim' and challenges the principle 'from one only one' often held by philosophers.

الفيض
