Defining Genus and Differentia and Refuting Infinite Regress
Defines genus and species and refutes infinite regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define genus and differentia and refute the objection that definition leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Genus and differentia are self-sufficient: the differentia does not need another differentia because it is unique.

In this passage, the author defines two essential components of definition: genus (jins) and differentia (fasl). He then tackles a classic objection: if a species is defined by its differentia, then that differentia would itself need a differentia, leading to an infinite regress. The refutation hinges on the fact that the differentia does not share its essential attributes with anything else, so it is self-distinguishing.

Guided Path
1
Define genus and differentia from the passage
Identify genus as the common part shared by multiple quiddities, and differentia as the distinguishing part. Note that their quiddities are distinct.
2
Explain why the quiddity of each is external to the other
The genus is perfection of the common part, and differentia is perfection of the distinguishing part. They are separate metaphysical constituents.
3
Reconstruct the infinite regress objection
Formulate the objection: If a species like man is defined by a differentia (e.g., rational), then that differentia itself would need a differentia to distinguish it from other differentiae, leading to an infinite chain.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author argues that a differentia does not share its essential attributes with any other thing; it is unique. Therefore, it does not need another differentia to distinguish it.
5
Test with an example
Apply to the definition of 'human': genus = animal, differentia = rational. Is rational self-distinguishing? Yes, because rationality is not shared by other species within the animal kingdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الجنس والنوع: إذا اشتركت بعض الماهيات في حقيقة فبما به الاشتراك مغاير لما به الامتياز. الأول هو الجنس، والثاني هو الفصل. ويظهر من هذا أن ماهية كل واحد من الجنس والفصل خارجة عن ماهية الآخر. فالجنس كمال الجزء المشترك، والفصل كمال الجزء المميز. وبه سقط شبهة من قال: لو كان الإنسان إنما يغني عن غيره بالفصل، لاحتاج الفصل في تميزه عن غيره إلى فصل، ولا إلى نهاية. لأنا نقول: إنما يحتاج إلى الفصل لمشاركته لشيء من الذاتيات، والفصل لا يشارك غيره في ذاتياته فلا يحتاج إلى فصل.
Topics of genus and species: When some quiddities share in a reality, then that by which they share is different from that by which they are distinguished. The first is genus, the second is differentia. And it appears from this that the quiddity of each of genus and differentia is external to the quiddity of the other. Thus genus is perfection of the common part, and differentia is perfection of the distinguishing part. And by this is refuted the doubt of one who said: If man is only distinct from others by the differentia, then the differentia would need another differentia to distinguish it from others, leading to infinity. For we say: It only needs a differentia because it shares something of the essential attributes, and the differentia does not share with others in its own essential attributes, so it does not need a differentia.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) in the context of definition (hadd). The author states that when multiple quiddities share a reality, the common part is genus and the distinguishing part is differentia. Their quiddities are distinct: genus is the perfection of the shared part, differentia is the perfection of the distinctive part. Then the author refutes a famous objection: if a species like man is defined by its differentia (e.g., rational), then that differentia itself would need a differentia to distinguish it from other differentiae, leading to infinite regress. The refutation is that a differentia does not share its essential attributes with any other thing; it is self-distinguishing. For example, 'rational' (natiq) does not share its essence with any other differentia, so it needs no further differentia. This is a key argument in Islamic logic used to defend the coherence of definition by genus and differentia.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): The genus is that which is common to multiple species, e.g., 'animal' for human and horse. [Note: In later logic, jins may also refer to a category of being; here it is strictly the genus in definition.]
فصل (fasl): The differentia that distinguishes one species from others within the same genus, e.g., 'rational' for human. [Note: No significant shift; consistent across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The author refutes the infinite regress argument by claiming that the differentia does not need another differentia because:
A) The differentia is simpler than the genus.
B) The differentia does not share its essential attributes with anything else.
C) The differentia is identical to the species.
D) The differentia is acquired by accident.

Key Takeaway
Genus and differentia are distinct metaphysical components of a definition, and the differentia is self-distinguishing, thus escaping infinite regress.

Defining Genus: The First of the Five Universals
Author defines genus and explains its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus from a definition and identify which parts of the definition exclude other universals.

Core Idea
Genus is the universal that answers 'what is it?' across species differing in essence.

In this passage, the author defines genus (jins) as one of the five universals. He carefully constructs the definition to exclude other universals like species, differentia, and accidental universal. Understanding this definition is essential for analyzing definitions in all Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components of the definition
The definition has three main parts: 'predicated of many', 'differing in realities', 'in answer to what is it?'. Each part serves to include genus and exclude other things.
2
Analyze what 'differing in realities' excludes
It excludes species (which differ only in accidents) and differentiate (which are not predicated of many). This ensures genus covers multiple distinct species.
3
Analyze what 'in answer to what is it' excludes
It excludes accidental universal (al-ard al-'amm) like 'white', because 'white' answers 'how is it?' not 'what is it?'.
4
Note the redundancy of 'universal' in the definition
The author notes that 'predicated of many' already implies universality, so adding 'kulli' is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجنس هو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالحقائق جواب ما هو. ويندرج فيه الخمسة. وقولنا 'مختلفين بالحقائق' يخرج الأنواع والفصول والخواص السافلة. وقولنا 'جواب ما هو' يخرج العرض العام. وهكذا التعريف منطبق على مطلق الجنس. ورسمه الشيخ في الإشارات بأنه كلي يُقال على أشياء مختلفة الحقائق جواب ما هو. لكن لا حاجة لذكر 'كلي' لأن المقول على الأشياء مرادف له.
The genus is that which is predicated of many things differing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'. And the five [universals] are included under it. Our saying 'differing in realities' excludes species, differentiate, and lower properties. Our saying 'in answer to what is it' excludes accidental universal. Thus the definition applies to absolute genus. And the Shaykh depicted it in al-Isharat as a universal predicated of things differing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'. But there is no need to mention 'universal' because 'predicated of things' is synonymous with it.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines genus (jins) as one of the five universals. The definition uses the phrase 'predicated of many differing in realities in answer to 'what is it?''. The author then explains the components: 'differing in realities' excludes species (which differ only in accidents) and differentiate (which are not predicated of many), and 'answer to what is it' excludes accidental universal (like 'white'). He then notes that this definition applies to absolute genus, and cites the definition from al-Isharat, but criticizes the addition of 'universal' as redundant. The passage is a standard definitional exposition common in logic texts.

Key Terms
الجنس
المقول على كثيرين
جواب ما هو

Key Takeaway

Definition of Genus and Its Four Types
Author defines genus and classifies it into supreme, intermediate, and subaltern types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish and classify the four types of genus based on the presence or absence of a superordinate and subordinate genus.

Core Idea
Genus is a universal predicated of many differing in essence; its types depend on hierarchy.

In this passage, the author presents the well-known definition of logical genus and then classifies it into four types: genus of genera, last genus, intermediate genus, and a third type that is omitted. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly using the five universals in definitions and for evaluating the logical structure of concepts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of genus
The author defines genus as a universal (kulli) that is predicated of many things differing in essence in answer to 'what is it?'. This distinguishes genus from species (predicated of one essence) and from accidents (not answering 'what is it?').
The definition uses 'many differing in essence' to exclude species and properties.
2
Identify the four types of genus
The author classifies genus based on whether it has a genus above it (superordinate) and a genus below it (subordinate). The four logical possibilities: (a) has above but not below: genus of genera (e.g., substance), (b) has below but not above: last genus (e.g., animal), (c) neither above nor below: omitted as impossible, (d) both above and below: intermediate genus (e.g., body).
The third type is impossible because if it has no genus above, it is not a species of anything; if no genus below, it is not a genus.
3
Apply the classification to examples from Islamic sciences
For instance, in the hierarchy of created beings, 'jawhar' (substance) is a genus of genera, 'jism' (body) is an intermediate genus, and 'hayawan' (animal) is a last genus. Practice identifying these in theological contexts.
Note that 'insan' (human) is a species under 'hayawan', not a genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشهور للجنس أنه المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالحقائق في جواب ما هو. فالمقبول كالجنر العيد. ثم الجنس إما أن لا يكون فوقه جنس ويكون تحته جنس، وهو المسمى جنس الأجناس. وإما أن لا يكون تحته جنس وفوقه جنس، وهو الجنس الأخير. وإما أن لا يكون فوقه جنس ولا تحته جنس، وهذا مما تركوه. وإما أن يكون فوقه جنس وتحته جنس، وهو المتوسط. ثم لا يجعل الجنس المطلق جنساً لهذه الأربعة، لأن الأول والثاني لاستقرار الأبارين أحدهما عدمي والمجموع المركب من العدم والثبوت لا يكون نوعاً للأم الشوني. وأما الثالث فليس إلا أصل الجنسية مع عدم سلب وصفين آخرين عنه فحون القول فيه كما فيهما. بقي الرابع لكن الله لا يصير.
The well-known (definition) for genus is that it is predicated of many things differing in essence in answer to 'what is it?'. So it is like the high genus. Then genus: either there is no genus above it and there is a genus below it—this is called the genus of genera; or there is no genus below it and there is a genus above it—this is the last genus; or there is no genus above it and no genus below it—this they left out; or there is a genus above it and a genus below it—this is the intermediate genus. Then one does not make the absolute genus a genus for these four, because the first and second are due to stability of the two aspects, one of which is privative and the composite of privation and affirmation does not become a species for the thing [?]. As for the third, it is nothing but the root of genusness together with the absence of two other attributes from it, so the statement about it is as about them. The fourth remains, but God does not make it.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the logical genus (al-jins al-mantiqi) and then classifies its types. The author begins by stating the standard definition: genus is a universal (kulli) that is predicated (maqul) of many things differing in essence (mukhtalifin bi'l-haqa'iq) in answer to 'what is it?' (fi jawab ma huwa). This definition excludes species, differentiae, properties, and accidents. For example, 'animal' is a genus for human, horse, and bird because each is asked 'what is it?' and answered 'animal', yet they differ in essence (humanity, horseness, etc.). After presenting the definition, the author addresses objections (not fully shown here) and then proceeds to classify genus into four kinds based on the presence or absence of a superordinate and subordinate genus: (1) genus of genera (jins al-ajnas) — e.g., substance (jawhar), which has no higher genus but has lower genera like body; (2) last genus (al-jins al-akhir) — e.g., animal, which has a higher genus (body) but no lower genera (since species are under it); (3) absolute genus with neither above nor below — this is considered impossible by logicians, so they omit it; (4) intermediate genus (al-jins al-mutawassit) — e.g., body, which has substance above and animal below. The author notes that these four are not all species of 'absolute genus' because the first and second involve a privation (lack of higher or lower), and a composite of existence and privation is not a proper species. The third type is merely the presence of genusness with two negations, so it is similar to the first two. The fourth type remains, but the author concludes with a brief remark. This classification is standard in Aristotelian logic as adapted by Muslim logicians.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): A universal predicated of many things differing in essence in answer to 'what is it?'; e.g., 'animal' for human and horse. [Note: Stable across the tradition; sometimes used for biological genus in non-logical contexts.]
المقول على كثيرين (al-maqul 'ala kathirin): Predicated of many individuals or species; a property of universals. [Note: Standard term; no significant shift.]
مختلفين بالحقائق (mukhtalifin bi'l-haqa'iq): Differing in essence or quiddity; the species under a genus share the genus but have distinct differentiae. [Note: Standard; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, which of the following is a valid example of 'genus'?
A) 'Human' said of Zayd and 'Amr.
B) 'Body' said of animal, plant, and mineral.
C) 'Rational' said of human.
D) 'White' said of milk and snow.

Key Takeaway
Genus is a universal predicated of many differing essences in answer to 'what is it?'; its types are distinguished by whether it has a higher genus, a lower genus, both, or neither.

The Four Kinds of Genus: Classification and Definition
Author lists the four kinds of genus and discusses the genus of genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four kinds of genus and determine whether a given genus is highest, lowest, intermediate, or individual.

Core Idea
Genus is a relational accident, not an essence—logical genus has no reality beyond being predicated of many.

In this passage, the author clarifies the nature of logical genus and provides a fourfold classification. Understanding this classification helps you navigate the hierarchy of genera in any definitional system, from substance down to species. We will parse the author's responses to objections and then extract the key division.

Guided Path
1
Separate the objection responses from the main classification
The passage begins with four answers to objections about the definition of genus. Identify these responses and set them aside; they clarify the definition but are not part of the classification proper.
The objections concern the meaning of 'predicated of many', 'constitutive parts', etc.
2
Extract the definition of logical genus
The author states that logical genus is 'predicated of many differing in reality in answer to what is it?'. Note that this is a relational accident, not an independent essence.
Contrast with natural genus which is the essence itself.
3
List the four kinds of genus
The author enumerates: (1) genus of genera (no genus above, genus below), (2) ultimate genus (genus above, no genus below), (3) individual (neither above nor below, omitted), (4) intermediate (both above and below). Write them down with their definitions.
The third kind is often omitted in practice.
4
Understand why the fourth kind is not a genus relative to a single species
The author argues that the intermediate genus cannot be a genus relative to only one species because a genus must be predicated of many species.
This clarifies that genus is essentially universal.
5
Analyze the ordering of predication
The author uses the example of 'body' and 'animal' being predicated of 'man'. Remote genera are predicated through intermediate genera, and priority in existence does not always align with priority in predication.
This shows the hierarchical nature of definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن الأوّل أن كون المقول على كثيرين جنسا للخير جنسية خاصة وهي مسافة عن مطلق الجنسية وذلك لا امتناع فيه وذلك لأن كل شخص من نوع متأخر بالطبع عن طبيعة ذلك النوع. وعن الثاني أنا نعني بالمقول الفرد المشترك من الأجزاء المقومة. وعن الثالث أن الجزء الذي هو الذات قد عمل به النوع في المواطاة كفرن الإنسان جوان؟ ولما سائر الأجناس فأناته العلى الجزء الذي هو الترات الأعلى المكتب؟ وعن الرابع أنا قد عنينا همنا شخص أن يكون جنسا، بل المقوم للنوع هو الماهية التي عرضت لها الجنسية. وزانت الخاسر؟ وهي أربعة: لأن الجنس إما أن لا يكون فوقه جنس ويكون تحته جنس وهو المسمى جنس الأجناس، وإما أن لا يكون تحته جنس وفوقه جنس وهو الجنس الأخير، وإما أن لا يكون فوقه جنس ولا تحته جنس وهذا الفنية مما تركوه، وإما أن يكون فوقه جنس وتحته جنس وهو المتوسط. ثم لا جعل الجنس المطلق جنسا لهذه الأربعة لأن الأول والثاني لا استقرادان؟ إلى النوع الواحد. ثم إن جعلنا هذه الأربعة أنواعا للجنس فينس الأجناس انتقم إلا إلى جنس أجناس هو جوهر وجذر الناس؟ هو كم وغيرهما. فإن ملنا أن اختلاف معروضات الأمور الإضافية تقضي بتوع الإضافات لم يكن جنس Concerning the first: that being predicated of many is a specific genus-ness for the other, and it is separate from absolute genus-ness, and there is no impossibility in that because every individual of a species is naturally posterior to the nature of that species. Concerning the second: we mean by 'predicated' the common individual of the constitutive parts. Concerning the third: that the part which is the essence has been used by the species in correspondence, like... and concerning the rest of the genera, its upper part is the part which is the higher level? Concerning the fourth: we intended... the least collection? This definition is a definition or a description according to the famous books: it is a descriptive definition, you say 'genus is such and such'. Know that this definition is for logical genus, and it has no essence beyond this extent, for there is no meaning of 'animal being a genus' except its being predicated of many differing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'. So it is a definition? Or a quasi-description? For it is only a description if logical genus had a reality different from this consideration and attached to it, but it is not so. Genus qua genus is not constitutive of the essence of species because being a genus is an accidental relation that occurs to natural genus in relation to species, so it is necessarily separate from the essence of species, and the opposite? is not a part, because if it were constitutive of species qua genus, then every species—indeed every individual—would have to be a genus. Rather, the constitutive of species is the essence to which genus-ness occurs. And its loss? are four: because genus either: there is no genus above it and there is a genus below it—this is called the genus of genera; or there is no genus below it and there is a genus above it—this is the ultimate genus; or there is no genus above it and no genus below it—this is an individual, which they omitted; or there is a genus above it and a genus below it—this is the intermediate. Then one cannot make absolute genus a genus for these four because the first and second are not... the two? one is privative and the composite of privation and affirmation cannot be a species for the... And as for the third, it is nothing but the root of genus-ness with the negation of two other descriptions, so the ruling about it is as about them. The fourth remains, but it does not become a genus in relation to one species. Then if we make these four species of genus, the genus of genera will only revert to a genus of genera which is substance, and the root of people? is quantity and others. If we incline that the difference of subjects of relational matters necessitates the differentiation of relations, then the genus of genera would not be a species for another, all and if... he mentioned, it would be an ultimate species, and above it is the genus, and above it is the predicated on many differing in realities, and above it is the predicated on many differing in actuality, and above it is the predicated on many by potentiality which is the... and above it is the relative, so the relative is the genus of genera. And the genus of genera is the species of species in the manner of ordering the excessive genera of predication on the species. And he mentioned the remote genus? ... for it is impossible for body to be predicated of man except after it becomes animal, because body that is not animal is not predicated of man. And because it is so, the predication of animal on him is prior to the predication of body. If it is said: the remote is part of the close genus, the part is prior to the whole due to its simplicity, so body is prior in existence to animal, we say: there are types in it, but there is no impossibility that the posterior to a thing in existence be a cause for the establishment of that prior for something else.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion about the nature and classification of genus (al-jins) in logic. It begins by answering four objections concerning the definition of genus as 'predicated of many differing in reality in answer to what it is'. The author clarifies that this definition is specific to logical genus, which does not have an independent essence beyond this relational consideration. Then the author enumerates four types of genus based on whether a genus exists above or below it: the genus of genera (jins al-ajnas) which has no genus above it but has a genus below, the ultimate genus (al-jins al-akhir) which has a genus above but no genus below, the individual (al-shakhs) which has neither above nor below (often omitted), and the intermediate genus (al-mutawassit) which has both above and below. The author then discusses why absolute genus cannot be a genus for these four types, focusing on the composite nature of the first two and the privative aspect of the third. The discussion continues on whether the four types themselves can be species of a higher genus, exploring the relationship of relational attributes to their subjects. Finally, the author examines the ordering of genera in predication, using the example of 'body' and 'animal' in relation to 'man'. The point is that remote genera are predicated through intermediate genera, and priority in existence does not always align with priority in predication.

Key Terms
الجنس
جنس الأجناس
الجنس الأخير
الجنس المتوسط

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Genus qua genus is not constitutive of the species; it is a relational accident predicated of many differing realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Two Meanings of Species and Their Distinction
Author distinguishes two meanings of species (naw') and clarifies their properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of species (naw') and identify their logical properties, including composition and relationship to genus.

Core Idea
Species has two meanings: infima species (no genus above) and subgenus species (composite of genus and differentia).

In the study of the five universals, species (naw') is a crucial intermediate between genus and individual. However, the term 'species' itself has two distinct meanings in logic, which this passage from al-Razi clarifies. Understanding this distinction is essential for properly analyzing definitions and hierarchies of concepts, especially when dealing with the structure of definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Pinpoint the two definitions
Locate in the Arabic the two meanings of 'species'. The first meaning (النوع بالمعنى الأول) is the universal predicated of many differing in number only (the infima species). The second meaning (النوع بالمعنى الثاني) is that which is predicated of others under a genus (any species under a higher genus).
The Arabic phrases are key: 'النوع بالمعنى الأول' and 'النوع بالمعنى الثاني'.
2
Compare their logical properties
List the differences: the first cannot be a genus (it is the lowest), the second can be a genus for lower species. The first is predicated directly of individuals, the second is subject to a higher genus. The first may be simple (if it has no differentia), the second is necessarily composite (genus + differentia).
3
Test with an example from Islamic sciences
Take 'salah' (prayer) as a species under the genus 'worship' (ibadah). 'Salah' is a species in the second meaning because it has a genus. But 'salah' itself can be a genus for types like fard salah and sunnah salah. Conversely, 'fard salah' might be an infima species if it has no further species. Apply both meanings to these terms.
This shows how the same term can be considered in both senses depending on perspective.
4
Reflect on the passage's conclusion
The passage ends by refuting the assumption that every species has a genus. Why? Because the highest genera (e.g., 'substance') when taken stripped of their differentiae are species in the first meaning and have no genus above them. So not every species falls under a genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage clarifies the two meanings of 'species' (al-naw') in logic. The first meaning is the universal predicated of many differing only in number (i.e., the infima species). The second meaning is that which is predicated of others under a genus—essentially any species that is not the highest. The author lists several differences: the first meaning cannot be a genus, while the second can be a genus for species below it. The first is predicated of what is below it (individuals), while the second is subject to what is above it (higher genus). The first may be simple (if it is the ultimate species), but the second is necessarily composite, composed of genus and differentia, because it falls under a genus and must be distinguished from coordinate species by a differentia. The passage ends by noting that the natures of genera, when stripped of their dividing additions, become species in the first meaning, and this refutes the claim that every species must have a genus, since the highest genera have no genus above them.

النوع بالمعنى الثاني

Real Species vs Relative Species
Distinguishing real and relative species

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real species (al-naw' al-haqiqi) and relative species (al-naw' al-mudaf) based on their definitions and functions in the hierarchy.

Core Idea
Real species is predicated of individuals; relative species is defined by its position above and below.

The five universals include species, but not all species are alike. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi distinguishes two kinds: the real species, which is one of the five universals and predicated of many differing in number only, and the relative species, which is defined by its place in the hierarchy of genera and species. Understanding this distinction is essential for using definitions correctly in Islamic sciences such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two definitions of species
Read the passage where species is defined: 'النوع تعريفا لأشتراك على الكلي المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالعدد فقط' (species is defined as the universal predicated of many differing in number only). This is the real species. Then note that the relative species is defined by its position: being below a genus and above a species.
The passage later clarifies that the relative species is not one of the five universals but a positional concept.
2
Identify the key difference: predication vs position
The real species is a 'predicated' universal (محمول) — it applies directly to its individuals. The relative species is a 'subject' (موضوع) — it is something that can have something predicated of it, and itself be predicated of something else in the hierarchy.
The passage states: 'النوع المحمول هو الحقيقي، أما المضاف فهو موضوع.'
3
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider 'human' (insan) as a real species: it is predicated of Zayd, Amr, etc., differing in number. As a relative species, 'human' is below 'animal' and above 'rational animal' (if considered). In usul al-fiqh, when defining 'riba', the genus (jins) is 'excess' or 'deferred payment', and the species (naw') is the specific type of excess — this is a relative species.
The real species is one of the five universals; the relative species is a logical tool for classification.
4
Test the distinction with an example
Given the claim that 'every real species is also a relative species', check against the passage: the author refutes this, saying each can be true where the other is false. For instance, 'animal' is a real species of 'man' but also a genus relative to 'man'? Actually, 'animal' is not a real species of 'man' (it is a genus). So the claim is false. Find a case: a real species like 'man' is also a relative species because it has a genus above it.
The author states: 'كل واحد منهما قد ا ا صدق على ما يكذب عليه الآخر' – each can be true where the other is false, e.g., in intermediate genera.

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two kinds of species: the real species (al-naw' al-haqiqi) and the relative species (al-naw' al-idafi, also called al-madaf). It also explains the hierarchy of genera and species, including the concept of jins al-ajnas (genus of genera) and naw' al-anwa' (species of species). The author begins by discussing genus in relation to species, stating that if we make four items species for a genus, then genera are confined except to the ultimate genus of genera, which is substance (jawhar), and the father of genera is quantity (kamm) and others. If we consider that differences in accidents of relative things necessitate different relations, then the genus of genera is not a final species. He then lists a hierarchy: a final species, above which is a series of higher genera, culminating in the relative (al-mudaf) which is the genus of genera. He mentions the impossibility of applying 'body' to 'man' except through 'animal', illustrating the logical priority of proximate genera. Addressing an objection that the remote part is prior due to simplicity, he replies that priority in existence does not prevent a later thing from being a cause for the subsistence of an earlier thing for another purpose. Moving to the discussion of species (mabahith al-naw'), he defines species in two senses: (1) the universal predicated of many differing in number only, and (2) that which is not predicated of anything else except the genus? The difference between species and genus is elaborated in several aspects: species in the first sense cannot be a genus, but in the second sense it can? Genus in the first sense may be simple, but in the second it is not, because everything falling under a genus with something else must have a distinguishing feature. The species in the second sense is composed of genus and differentia, while the first sense refers to the natures of universal genera stripped of their dividing differentiae. The author then refutes the claim that every real species is a relative species, explaining that each can be true where the other is false: the relative species may apply where the real does not in intermediate genera, and vice versa for simple vs. compound natures. He asserts that the species among the five universals is the real species, not the relative, because the five are universals and the predicated species is real, while the relative is a subject (position). He lists the layers of real species (varying by level) and relative species (four layers: highest, intermediate, high, last). The discussion of whether a species can be a genus parallels that in genus. For naw' al-anwa' (species of species), it is called species equivocally in three ways: (1) as predicated of many differing in number, making it real; (2) as having a genus above it; and (3) as being composed. The passage concludes that a species can have a single individual, and philosophers even posit species without current individuals. This clarifies the logical hierarchy and the need for both real and relative species.

Key Terms
النوع الحقيقي
النوع المضاف
الكلي
الحمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The relative species is one of the five universals, while the real species is not.
D) There is no distinction; they are the same.

Key Takeaway
The real species is a essential universal; the relative species is a positional concept; they are not interchangeable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Hierarchy of Genera and Species
Explains hierarchy of genera and species

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four layers of relative species and explain how naw' al-anwa' (species of species) is used equivocally.

Core Idea
Relative species has four layers; naw' al-anwa' is said in three ways.

The passage not only distinguishes real from relative species but also lays out the hierarchical structure of genera and species. The relative species comes in four layers based on what is above and below it. Furthermore, the term 'species of species' (naw' al-anwa') is used in three distinct senses: as a real species, as a relative species with a genus above, and as a composition. This hierarchical understanding is crucial for constructing sound definitions and classifications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four layers of relative species
Read the part: 'النوع المضاف فله مراتب أربع: لأن النوع إما أن يكون فوقه نوع ولا تحته نوع ... وإما أن يكون فوقه نوع وتخته نوع وهو المتوسط ... إما أن لا يكون فوقه نوع وتحته نوع وهو النوع العالي ... إما بالعكس وهو الأخير.' Recognize the four types: (1) highest (above it a species, below none), (2) intermediate (above and below), (3) high (no above, has below), (4) last (opposite of high).
These layers depend on the immediate hierarchy, not on absolute position.
2
Understand the equivocal use of 'species of species' (naw' al-anwa')
The passage says: 'في نوع الأنواع يقال عليه النوع بالاشتراك من ثلاثة أوجه' – species of species is said equivocally in three ways. First, as predicated of many differing in number (real species). Second, as having a genus above it (so it is a relative species). Third, as a composition of the two? The passage is unclear due to corruption, but the idea is that the same term can refer to different logical concepts.
This shows the need to clarify which sense is meant when using 'naw' al-anwa'.
3
Apply the hierarchy to a real example
Consider the Porphyrian tree: substance (jins al-ajnas) -> body -> living -> animal -> human. 'Human' is a real species (infima species). It is also a relative species: it has 'animal' above it (genus) and no species below it (unless we consider races, but logically none). So it is the 'last' layer? Actually, it is the species below which no species exist. According to the four layers, human is the 'last' (الأخير) because it has a genus above it and no species below.
In the relative sense, human is not the highest; it is the lowest in that branch.
4
Explain why a species can have one individual
The passage concludes: 'قد عرفت أنه لا نصير جنسا إلا بنوعين. فاعلم الآن أنه يكفي كون الشيء نوعا أن يكون له شخص واحد' – a genus requires at least two species, but a species suffices with one individual. This is important for theological concepts like 'the world' or 'God' which may be unique species.
This principle allows for species with a single instance in kalam.

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two kinds of species: the real species (al-naw' al-haqiqi) and the relative species (al-naw' al-idafi, also called al-madaf). It also explains the hierarchy of genera and species, including the concept of jins al-ajnas (genus of genera) and naw' al-anwa' (species of species). The author begins by discussing genus in relation to species, stating that if we make four items species for a genus, then genera are confined except to the ultimate genus of genera, which is substance (jawhar), and the father of genera is quantity (kamm) and others. If we consider that differences in accidents of relative things necessitate different relations, then the genus of genera is not a final species. He then lists a hierarchy: a final species, above which is a series of higher genera, culminating in the relative (al-mudaf) which is the genus of genera. He mentions the impossibility of applying 'body' to 'man' except through 'animal', illustrating the logical priority of proximate genera. Addressing an objection that the remote part is prior due to simplicity, he replies that priority in existence does not prevent a later thing from being a cause for the subsistence of an earlier thing for another purpose. Moving to the discussion of species (mabahith al-naw'), he defines species in two senses: (1) the universal predicated of many differing in number only, and (2) that which is not predicated of anything else except the genus? The difference between species and genus is elaborated in several aspects: species in the first sense cannot be a genus, but in the second sense it can? Genus in the first sense may be simple, but in the second it is not, because everything falling under a genus with something else must have a distinguishing feature. The species in the second sense is composed of genus and differentia, while the first sense refers to the natures of universal genera stripped of their dividing differentiae. The author then refutes the claim that every real species is a relative species, explaining that each can be true where the other is false: the relative species may apply where the real does not in intermediate genera, and vice versa for simple vs. compound natures. He asserts that the species among the five universals is the real species, not the relative, because the five are universals and the predicated species is real, while the relative is a subject (position). He lists the layers of real species (varying by level) and relative species (four layers: highest, intermediate, high, last). The discussion of whether a species can be a genus parallels that in genus. For naw' al-anwa' (species of species), it is called species equivocally in three ways: (1) as predicated of many differing in number, making it real; (2) as having a genus above it; and (3) as being composed. The passage concludes that a species can have a single individual, and philosophers even posit species without current individuals. This clarifies the logical hierarchy and the need for both real and relative species.

Key Terms
النوع المضاف
مراتب أربع
نوع الأنواع
الاشتراك

Relative species has four layers; naw' al-anwa' is used in three senses; a species can exist with a single individual.

Real Species vs Additive Species
Author distinguishes real species from additive species and identifies real species as one of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the real species (النوع الحقيقي) and the additive species (النوع المضاف) and explain why the real species is one of the five universals.

Core Idea
Real species is predicated directly; additive species is relational and not a universal.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas clarifies a crucial distinction in the theory of definition: the real species versus the additive species. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the five universals, especially in defining terms in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key terms
Locate in the Arabic the terms النوع الحقيقي (real species) and النوع المضاف (additive species). Note their literal meanings: 'haqiqi' means true/real, 'mudaf' means relational/additional.
The passage begins with 'ليس كل نوع حقيقي هو نوع مضاف' — not every real species is an additive species.
2
Understand the overlap and difference
The author states that each can be true where the other is false. For example, additive species can apply to intermediate genera where real species does not. This shows they are not coextensive.
Look for 'اما ان المضاف قد يصدق حيث يكذب الحقيقي' — the additive may be true where the real is false.
3
Analyze simple vs composite essences
Simple essences (بسائط) are always real species, not additive. Composite essences lead back through their components until a real species is reached. This reinforces that real species are fundamental.
The text says: 'فأن كانت بسائط فكل واحد منها نوع حقيقي وليس مضاف' — if they are simple, each is a real species, not additive.
4
Connect to the five universals
The five universals (الكليات الخمس) are predicated of many. The species that is predicated without mediation is the real species. The additive species, being relative, is not one of the five.
The passage concludes: 'النوع الذي هو أحد الخمسة هو الحقيقي لا المضاف' — the species that is one of the five is the real, not the additive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Every real species is not an additive species, nor is the truth rough? Both each of them may be true of what the other is false of. But the additive may be true where the real is false in intermediate genera, and the opposite? For the essences are states that are simple or composite. If they are simple, then each of them is a real species and not additive, and the two? the council? that leads to a contradiction. If they are composites, they are unavoidably composed? and we return to what we mentioned: that the species which is one of the five is real, not additive, because the five are universals? and the species is predicated without mediation, and the predicated species is the real one. As for the additive, it is the subject of the ranks of species.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between real species (نوع حقيقي) and additive species (نوع مضاف). The author states that each can be true where the other is false, especially in intermediate genera. He argues that simple essences are real species, while composite ones lead to the conclusion that the real species is one of the five universals (الكليات الخمس). The real species is predicated directly, while the additive species is subject to hierarchical ranking. This establishes that the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) are the real species, not the relational additive species.

Key Terms
نوع حقيقي (naw' haqiqi) — real species, one of the five universals, predicated of many without mediation.
نوع مضاف (naw' mudaf) — additive species, relative to another species in a hierarchy, not a universal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The real species is predicated directly and is one of the five universals; the additive species is relational and not counted among them.

The Four Ranks of Additive Species
Author enumerates the four possible ranks of additive species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given species into one of the four ranks of additive species based on whether it has species above and below it.

Core Idea
Additive species take four positions in a hierarchy: highest, intermediate, lowest, and isolated.

The concept of additive species (النوع المضاف) is crucial for organizing knowledge hierarchically. In this passage, the author lists the four possible relationships a species can have with ranks above and below it. Understanding these ranks helps you precisely locate any species within a classification tree, essential for defining terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept being classified
The passage classifies 'al-naw' al-mudaf' (additive species). Recognize that this is a species considered in relation to other species in a hierarchy.
2
Recognize the two criteria
The classification uses two binary criteria: whether the species has a species above it, and whether it has a species below it. This yields 4 combinations.
3
List the four combinations
Write out the 2x2 table: (above, below), (above, no below), (no above, below), (no above, no below). Match each to the author's description.
4
Map each combination to the author's rank
According to the passage: (1) above, no below → like a genus with no species below; (2) above and below → intermediate; (3) no above, below → highest species; (4) no above, no below → the last (lowest/isolated).
The fourth rank is described as 'the opposite of that', meaning the opposite of the third's conditions.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take a hierarchy from fiqh, aqida, or grammar and classify a species into one of the four ranks. For instance, in usul al-fiqh, 'wajib' has 'fard' above and 'sunna' below, making it intermediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اما المضاف فله مراتب اربع لان النوع إما ان الكون فوقه نوع ولا تحته نوع وهو كما اذا فرضنا جنا لين عاليا عنه انواع ولا نواعه انواع واما از يكون فوقه نوع و تخته نوع وهو المتوسط واما ان لا تكون فوقه نوت وتحته نوع وهو النوع العالى وانا بالعكس و هو الأخير
As for the additive species, it has four ranks: because the species either has a species above it and no species below it – like when we posit two genera above it, and it has no species of its own; or it has a species above it and a species below it – and it is the intermediate; or it has no species above it and a species below it – and it is the highest species; and the opposite of that, which is the last.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four ranks of additive species. Additive species are considered in relation to other species. The four possibilities: (1) a species above it, none below (like a genus that is not a species of anything else); (2) a species both above and below (intermediate); (3) no species above, but a species below (the highest species); (4) the opposite of the third, which is the last. The fourth rank likely means no species above and none below, but the text is ambiguous. This classification allows one to place any species in a hierarchy.

Key Terms

النوع المضاف
المتوسط
الاخير

Checkpoint

B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Additive species have four possible hierarchical positions: highest, intermediate, lowest, and isolated, determined by presence or absence of species above and below.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Condition for Being a Species: One Individual Suffices
Author clarifies the meaning of species of species and the condition for being a species

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for something to be a species and distinguish between the different ways the term 'species' is used.

Core Idea
A species needs only one individual, and 'species of species' is homonymous.

This passage from al-Mulakhkhas tackles a subtle point: what does it take for something to be a species? Is it necessary to have many individuals? The author clarifies that a single individual suffices, and he also unpacks the term 'species of species' (naw' al-anwa'), showing that it is called 'species' only by common naming. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly applying the five universals in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term 'species of species'
Find the phrase 'نوع الأنواع' in the Arabic. Note that it refers to the highest species, which is actually a genus in relation to lower species.
2
Identify the three aspects of homonymy
The author says 'species of species' is called species by common naming (بالاشتراك) from three aspects. Try to extract the first two: (1) it is predicated of many differing only in number, (2) it has a genus above it. The third is unclear due to corruption.
Homonymy means the same word is used with different meanings.
3
Understand the condition for being a species
The author states explicitly: 'يكفى كون الشيء نوعا أن يكون له شخص واحد' – it suffices for a thing to be a species to have one individual. This means a species does not require multiple instantiations.
This is a critical refinement: contrary to common assumption, a single individual can define a species.
4
Connect to Islamic logical practice
Reflect on why this matters: in Islamic logic (mantiq), definitions (hadd) rely on genus and species. This condition ensures that a species can be defined even if only one instance exists, e.g., a specific angel or celestial body.

Section: The connections of the species – is it here a genus? The discussion is like its like in the genus regarding the species of species. It is called species by common naming from three aspects: because it must be predicated of many differing only in number, so it is a real species from this aspect. And it must have a genus above it that is a species – that is the species of species – so it is a species for them from the four together from this aspect. Then its being species of species is for each of these two aspects? ... and it is known that the word applying to each of the two simple things and to what you combine? is not except by common naming in that it suffices for a thing to be a species to have one individual. You have known that a thing does not become a genus except by two ??? So now know that it suffices for a thing to be a species to have one individual. Rather, the wise have used species that have no individuals now among the common investigations between genus and species.
Additive species.
Species of species.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: the meaning of 'species of species' (نوع الأنواع) and the condition for something to be a species. The author asks whether the connection of species to genus is itself a genus. He explains that 'species of species' is called species by common naming from three aspects: (1) it is predicated of many differing only in number, so it is a real species from that aspect; (2) it must have a genus above it that is also a species, so it is a species in relation to the genera above it; (3) the third aspect is unclear. Then he states that the word 'species' applied to these is only by common naming (homonymy) in that it suffices for a thing to be a species if it has a single individual. He affirms that a species need not have multiple individuals; a single instance suffices, and the wise have indeed used species that have no existing individuals now. The passage ends with the headings 'Additive species' and 'Species of species'.

Natural, Logical, and Intellectual Genus: Why Logical Genus Is Not Constitutive
Author explains differences between natural, logical, and intellectual genus/species and argues logical genus is not constitutive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish natural, logical, and intellectual genus and explain why logical genus is not constitutive of natural or logical species.

Core Idea
Logical genus is a relative accident; it does not constitute the essence of a species.

In defining things, we often use 'genus' and 'differentia'. But not every genus is a real part of the essence. The author of the Mulakkhkhas distinguishes three kinds of genus: natural, logical, and intellectual. He argues that the logical genus is merely a relative accident that cannot constitute the natural species. This distinction is crucial for understanding definitions in Islamic logic and avoiding conceptual confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of genus
Find the definitions of natural, logical, and intellectual genus in the passage. Natural genus is the common essence existing externally; logical genus is a relative accident that attaches to the natural genus in relation to species; intellectual genus is the intelligible form before multiplicity.
These correspond to the three aspects of universals: before, with, and after multiplicity.
2
Extract the two reasons why logical genus is not constitutive
The author gives two reasons: (1) The natural species (e.g., man) is non-additive (its essence is not relational), while logical genus is additive (it is a relation); the non-additive cannot constitute the additive. (2) Logical genus is a relative accident that attaches to the natural genus; accidents cannot constitute essences.
The same reasoning applies to logical species and logical differentia.
3
Understand the three aspects of universals
The author describes genus and species in three states: before multiplicity (the intellect form), with multiplicity (the common nature in individuals), and after multiplicity (the abstract concept after observation). This is the Avicennan framework.
These are not different types but three considerations of the same universal.
4
Compare genus and species using the passage
The author quotes Avicenna that the highest genera (genera of genera) are more than one but finite, while the lowest species (species of species) are finite in nature but infinite potentially. Genus exceeds species in generality; species exceeds genus in definition because it includes both genus and differentia.
This concludes the lesson by showing the logical relations between genus and species.

These are five? You already know the difference between natural, logical, and intellectual [genus/species] after resolving the difference between natural, logical, and intellectual genus, and similarly species and differentia. The logical genus is not constitutive of the natural species nor of the logical species. As for the first: because the natural species is man for example, and the concept of genus is additional to the concept of man? Because first, man insofar as he is man is non-additive, while genus insofar as it is genus is additive, and the non-additive does not constitute the additive. Second, because the logical genus is a relative accident that attaches to the natural genus in relation to the natural species, and accidents are separate from the additive? So the logical genus is an obstacle to the realization of the natural species? As for the second: because the logical differentia and the logical genus are two states between the two collections? one from one aspect, and opposites do not constitute one another. So among the genus before multiplicity, the one with multiplicity, and after multiplicity, and similarly the species before multiplicity meaning the intellectual form of the emanated separables. And when the essence is found externally, the common factor among individuals is the one with multiplicity and in them. Then when man observes them and obtains in his mind upon observation a universal abstract, that is the one after multiplicity. And this consideration also applies to the five. The Shaykh said: The things that are in nature, the genera of genera are more than one but finite. As for the species of species, those preserved in nature are finite. But they are in themselves infinite potentially. For the species of species are many from the categories, infinite, like the species of species of quantity, quality, and posture. The genus exceeds the species in one respect and the species exceeds the genus in another. The first is by generality because the genus contains the species? The second is in the definition because the essence of the species includes the essence of the genus and includes the essence of the differentia. And the differentia in the investigations of differentia and hylomorphism? As also the constitution of the designated species contains the essence of the species.

Commentary
This passage begins by recalling the reader's prior knowledge of the differences between natural, logical, and intellectual genera and species. The author argues that the logical genus is not constitutive (مقوم) of either the natural species or the logical species. Two reasons are given: (1) The natural species (e.g., man) is non-additive (غير مضاف) in its essence, while the logical genus is additive (مضاف) because it is a relation; the non-additive cannot constitute the additive. (2) The logical genus is a relative accident that attaches to the natural genus; accidents cannot constitute essences. Regarding the logical species, the logical differentia and logical genus are two states that are opposites and cannot constitute each other. The author then explains the three aspects of genus and species: before multiplicity (قبل الكثرة) – the intelligible form; with multiplicity (مع الكثرة) – the common nature in individuals; after multiplicity (بعد الكثرة) – the abstract concept in the mind. He quotes Avicenna (الشيخ) stating that the highest genera (أجناس الأجناس) are more than one but finite; the lowest species (أنواع الأنواع) are finite in nature but infinite potentially. Finally, he compares genus and species: genus exceeds species in generality, while species exceeds genus in definition because it includes both genus and differentia.

Natural vs Logical Genus and Species
Author distinguishes natural from logical genus and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between natural and logical genus/species and explain why logical genus does not constitute natural species.

Core Idea
Logical genus is a relative accident, not a constituent of natural species.

The five universals include both natural and logical senses. This lesson clarifies the critical distinction between natural genus/species, which exist in reality, and logical genus/species, which are mental relations. Understanding this prevents confusion in defining essences across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinctions
The passage first distinguishes natural genus from logical genus, then natural species from logical species. Also note the triple division: before multiplicity, with multiplicity, after multiplicity.
2
Extract the argument for non-constitution
The author gives two reasons: (1) natural species is non-relative while logical genus is relative; the non-relative cannot constitute the relative. (2) logical genus is an accident posterior to the natural species.
Understand the logical priority: accidents depend on substances.
3
Analyze the three modes
Before multiplicity: intelligible form in the divine mind. With multiplicity: common element in external individuals. After multiplicity: abstract concept in the mind. This applies to all five universals.
4
Test with an example
Take 'man' as natural species and 'animal' as natural genus. The logical genus 'animal' is a relative predicate. Does it constitute 'man'? No, because 'man' as such is non-relative.
5
Apply the triple division to other universals
Consider 'substance' as a genus. Its before-multiplicity form is the intelligible substance; with-multiplicity is the common essence in individuals; after-multiplicity is the mental concept. Verify each mode.

They are five. The difference between the natural genus and the logical genus, and the natural species and the logical species. The logical genus is not constitutive of the natural species nor of the logical species. As for the first, because the natural species is, for example, man, and the concept of genus-ness is not part of the concept of man, because man insofar as he is man is non-relative, and genus insofar as it is genus is relative, and the non-relative does not constitute the relative. As for the second, because the logical genus is a relative state that occurs to the natural genus relative to the natural species, and accidents are posterior to the relatives, so the logical genus is separable from the realization of the natural species. As for the second [i.e., the logical species], because the logical species and the logical genus are two relative states, and two opposites do not constitute each other. Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between natural and logical genus and species, a common topic in Islamic logic. The author begins by stating that the logical genus does not constitute either the natural or the logical species. The reason is twofold: first, the natural species (like 'man') is non-relative, while the logical genus (the universal 'animal') is relative; the non-relative cannot constitute the relative. Second, the logical genus is an accidental relation that attaches to the natural genus; accidents depend on their subjects and are posterior, so the logical genus can be absent even when the natural species is present. Similarly, the logical species and logical genus are mutually relative opposites and cannot constitute each other. The passage then clarifies three modes of genus and species: before multiplicity (the intelligible form in the divine mind), with multiplicity (the common element in external individuals), and after multiplicity (the abstract concept in the mind). This triple division applies to all five universals.

Differentia as cause and the objection answered
Author discusses whether differentia is a cause for the species' existence, presents Shaykh's view, objection, and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the debate on whether differentia is a cause of the species and explain how the objection is answered by distinguishing between the need for a cause and the specification of the cause.

Core Idea
The differentia's causal role depends on the cause's specification, not the effect's dependence.

In defining a species, the differentia plays a crucial role. Ibn Sina went further to claim that the differentia is a cause of the species' existence. This raised a logical objection: if differentia is an attribute, it depends on the species, so how can it be the cause? In this passage, the author presents the objection and a subtle response that clarifies the nature of causal relations and definitional predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three considerations of the differentia
The author lists three relations: to absolute genus (divides it), to species (part of it), and to the species' share of the genus (constitutive). Understand each to see the context.
This sets the stage for the causal claim.
2
Locate the Shaykh's claim and the objection
Ibn Sina says the differentia must be a cause of the species. The objection: the differentia may be an attribute, and an attribute needs a subject (the species), so it cannot be a cause of something it depends on.
The objection seems plausible.
3
Analyze the response
The response distinguishes: the effect (species) always needs some cause, but which specific cause it is is determined by the cause itself, not by the effect. Thus the differentia's dependence does not preclude it from being the cause.
This is a subtle move that preserves the causal role.
4
Clarify the predication of differentia in 'what is it?'
The author notes that the differentia qua differentia is not a predicate in answer to 'what is it?', but the quiddity that happens to be a differentia can be. Similarly for the genus. This resolves confusion about definition.
This connects to the theory of definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر الفصل. للفصل اعتبارات: بالقياس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية المطلقة فيكون مقسماً لها، وبالقياس إلى النوع فيكون جزءاً له، وبالقياس إلى حصة النوع من الجنس فيكون مقوماً لها. وذهب الشيخ إلى أن الفصل يجب أن يكون علة لوجود النوع. وعند ذلك إشكال: لأن الفصل قد يكون صفة والصفة محتاجة إلى الموصوف، والمحتاج إلى الغير لا يكون علة له. بل قد يكون الأمر كذلك على تفصيل. الجواب: أن المعلول ما هو هو محتاج إلى علة ما، فلا جرم أبداً يكون محتاجاً إلى العلة. فأما تعيين العلة فليس من جانب المعلول بل من جانب العلة. وأما أن الفصل المقوم للنوع لا يمكن أن يكون مقولاً بالنسبة إلى النوع في جواب ما هو البتة، وأن الجنس لا يمكن أن يكون صفة لا بالنسبة إلى جواب ما هو. والحق: إن كان المراد الفصل حيث هو فصل لا يكون مقولاً في جواب ما هو، والجنس حيث هو جنس لا يكون مقولاً في جواب ما هو، فضلاً لأنه إنما يكون فصلاً من حيث إنه يميز شيئاً عن شيء. وأما إن كان المراد الماهية التي عرضت لها الفصلية فلا يستحيل أن يكون الفصل مقولاً في جواب ما هو. ولو كان الفصلا يستحيل أن يصير مقولاً في جواب ما هو، وبالعكس فهو خطأ. فإذا كانت الحقيقة مركبة من أمرين كل واحد منهما أعم من الآخر من وجه وأخص من وجه، فإن كان واحد من جزئية مقولاً في جواب ما هو تارة.
Mention of the differentia. The differentia has considerations: with respect to the absolute generic nature, it divides it; with respect to the species, it is a part of it; with respect to the share of the species from the genus, it constitutes it. And the Shaykh held that the differentia must be a cause for the existence of the species. There is an objection to this: because the differentia may be an attribute, and an attribute requires a subject, and that which requires something else cannot be a cause of it. But it may be so upon a detailed account. The answer: the effect, as it is, is in need of some cause, so necessarily it is always in need of a cause. As for the specification of the cause, it is not from the side of the effect but from the side of the cause. As for the differentia that constitutes the species, it cannot be predicated in answer to 'what is it?' with respect to the species at all, and that the genus cannot be an attribute with respect to the answer to 'what is it?' The truth: if what is meant is the differentia qua differentia, it is not said in answer to 'what is it?', and the genus qua genus is not said in answer to 'what is it?', let alone because it is a differentia only insofar as it distinguishes one thing from another. But if what is meant is the quiddity that has the property of being a differentia, then it is not impossible for the differentia to be said in answer to 'what is it?'. And if the differentia were impossible to become a predicate in answer to 'what is it?', and vice versa, that would be wrong. So when the reality is composed of two things each of which is more general than the other in one respect and more specific in another, then one of the two parts may be said in answer to 'what is it?' at times.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage discusses the differentia (al-faṣl) and its relationship to genus and species, particularly the view of Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) that the differentia is a cause for the existence of the species. The author first notes three considerations of the differentia: relative to the absolute generic nature (divides it), relative to the species (part of it), and relative to the species' share of the genus (constitutive). Then he presents Ibn Sina's claim: the differentia must be a cause of the species. An objection is raised: the differentia is often an attribute (ṣifa), and an attribute depends on its subject, so it cannot be a cause of something else that is prior. The response distinguishes: the effect (species) needs some cause, but the specific cause is not determined by the effect; it is from the side of the cause. The passage then clarifies that the differentia qua differentia is not a predicate in answer to 'what is it?' (jālib mā hūwa), and the genus qua genus is also not a predicate in answer to 'what is it?' (but rather 'what kind?'). However, if the quiddity happens to play the role of differentia, it can be so predicated. The point is that when a reality is composed of two things that are each more general or specific in some respect, one of the parts may sometimes be the answer to 'what is it?'.

Checkpoint

A) Because the differentia is not an attribute.
B) Because the effect always needs some cause, but the specific cause is determined by the cause, not the effect.
C) Because the differentia is not a cause at all.
D) Because the genus is the cause.

Key Takeaway
The differentia can be a cause of the species despite being an attribute, because the specification of the cause is independent of the effect's need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a definition of 'zakāt' in fiqh by identifying its genus and differentia. Then analyse whether the differentia can be considered a cause of the obligation of zakāt, using the points from this debate.
?
Consider the objection: 'The differentia depends on the species, so it cannot be a cause.' Write a response in your own words based on the author's answer.

Understanding Relative Genus and Differentia
Author provides an example to show that genus and differentia are relative, then refutes the claim that differentia causes genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the same term can serve as genus or differentia depending on the distinguishing context, and refute the claim that differentia causes the genus.

Core Idea
Genus and differentia are roles defined by context, not inherent properties.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses the simple example of 'animal' and 'white' to demonstrate a profound point: whether a term is a genus or differentia depends entirely on what we are distinguishing. He then dismantles an objection that the differentia cannot be a genus because it causes the genus. By the end, you will see how the five universals are not fixed labels but functional descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms in the example
The passage uses 'animal' and 'white'. Each is more general in one way and more specific in another: 'animal' is more general than 'white' regarding color varieties, but 'white' is more general than 'animal' regarding kinds of substances.
2
Consider the first distinguishing context
When distinguishing the composite of animal and white from non-white, 'animal' is the genus because it includes both white and non-white animals. 'White' becomes the differentia as it specifies which animal.
3
Consider the second distinguishing context
When distinguishing the composite from non-animal, 'white' becomes the genus (includes white animals and white non-animals) and 'animal' becomes the differentia. This shows the relativity of roles.
4
Examine the refutation of the causal claim
The author rejects the argument that the differentia causes the genus. Instead, the differentia divides the genus into species (divider) and constitutes the species (constituent). No causal relationship exists.
The differentia is muqassim (divider) for the genus and muqawwim (constituent) for the species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و جواب إيجا هو اخرى كالحيوان والأبيض فان كل واحد منها اعم من الآخر من وجه واخص من وجه فالحقيقة المركبة منها اذا اردنا تمييزها عن غير الأبيض كان الحيوان جنسا والابيض فصلا وإن أردنا تمييزها عن غير الحيوان كان الابيض جنسا والحيوان فصلا. و تعويل المانعين على ان الفصل علة لوجود الجنس والجنس معلول له والعلة لا تطلب معلولا فقد عرفت فساده. و الفصل بالنسبة الى الجنس مقسم وإلى النوع مقوم.
And the answer is another way, like 'animal' and 'white': each of them is more general than the other in one respect and more specific in another respect. So the composite reality of them, if we want to distinguish it from non-white, then 'animal' is genus and 'white' is differentia; and if we want to distinguish it from non-animal, then 'white' is genus and 'animal' is differentia. And the reliance of the prohibiters (i.e., those who deny that the differentia can be genus) upon the fact that the differentia is a cause of the existence of the genus, and the genus is an effect of it, and the cause does not seek the effect—you have known its corruption. And the differentia in relation to the genus is a divider, and in relation to the species is a constituent.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refinement: it first gives a concrete example to show that the roles of genus and differentia are not fixed but relative to what we are distinguishing. The example is the composite of animal and white. Each term is more general in one way and more specific in another: 'animal' is more general than 'white' in the sense that animal includes many colors, but 'white' is more general than 'animal' in the sense that white includes many non-animal things. So if we consider the composite 'white animal', and we want to distinguish it from whatever is not white, then 'animal' serves as the genus because it includes white animals and non-white animals, while 'white' serves as the differentia because it picks out the specific shade. Conversely, if we want to distinguish the composite from whatever is not an animal, then 'white' functions as the genus (since white includes animals and non-animals) and 'animal' functions as the differentia. This shows that the terms are not intrinsically genus or differentia; their classification depends on the context. The author then refutes the claim of those who say the differentia cannot be a predicate in 'what is it?' because they argue the differentia is a cause of the genus. The author says this has been refuted, and he clarifies: the differentia is a divider of the genus into its species, and it is a constituent of the species itself. This distinction is crucial: the differentia's relation to the genus is divisive, not causal. It does not cause the genus to exist; rather, it determines which species of the genus we are dealing with.

Key Terms
الفصل
الجنس
النوع
مقسم
مقوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's example, if we want to distinguish the composite of animal and white from non-white, which term functions as the genus?
A) White
B) Animal
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Genus and differentia are not intrinsic properties of terms but functional roles determined by the context of distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'qiyam' (standing) in prayer. Using the same logic as the animal-white example, show how 'qiyam' could be a genus if distinguishing prayer from non-standing things, or a differentia if distinguishing 'salah' from other acts of worship.
?
Identify the objection the author refutes: someone says 'differentia cannot be genus because it causes the genus.' Explain in one sentence why this objection fails, using the author's distinction between divider and constituent.
