Objections to Fasl as Cause: The Simple Cause Objection
Author presents objections and refutations regarding fasl as cause of genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that a simple cause cannot produce a composite effect, using the author's argument.

Core Idea
A simple cause can produce a composite effect; the differentia is not necessarily simple.

In this passage, the author engages in dialectic with opponents who deny that the differentia (fasl) can be a cause for the genus. We focus on the first two objections: the claim that a cause does not seek its effect, and that a simple cause cannot produce a composite effect. The author rejects both premises, defending the causal role of the differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
Find in the Arabic the statement: 'the cause does not seek an effect' (العلة لا تطلب معلولا). This is an opposition to the differentia being a cause for the genus.
2
Analyze the author's refutation of the first objection
The author says this objection is already known to be corrupt (فساده). He does not elaborate further, indicating the objection is weak.
3
Examine the second objection and refutation
The opponents say a simple cause cannot produce a composite effect. The author responds by denying the premise: 'we do not concede that a simple cause does not produce an effect' (لا نسلم أن العلة البسيطة لا يصدر عنها أثر). He also denies that the differentia is a cause for the specific share (حصة).
4
Apply the refutation to a similar argument
Consider an opponent objecting that God, being simple, cannot create a composite world. Using the author's method, you would deny that simplicity prevents causation, citing the same principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعويل المانعين على أن الفصل علة لوجود الجنس والجنس معلول له والعلة لا تطلب معلولا فقد عرفت فساده. وأما قولهم إن العلة البسيطة لا يصدر عنها أثر فإنه موجب عليهم أن يجعلوا الفصل مركبا. وأما نحن فلا نسلم أن العلة البسيطة لا يصدر عنها أثر، ولا نسلم أن الفصل علة للحصة. وأما قولهم إن الفصل لو كان علة لوجب أن يكون كل فصل مقوما، وليس كذلك لأن الفصل قد يكون مقسما غير مقوم، وأنه لو كان علة لعلل الشيء الواحد بعلتين، فالجواب: أما الأول فكون الفصل مقوما من وجه ومقسما من وجه، وأما الثاني فإن الفصل الذي هو كمال المميز لا يكون إلا واحدا، فليس ثم علتان. وأما قول الشيخ: إن الفصل علة لوجود الحصة والمعلول الواحد بالشخص لا يكون له علتان مستقلتان، فجوابنا: أن المجموع بالحقيقة فصل واحد وكل واحد جزء منه. وقولهم أيضا: إن طبيعة الجنس لو قعدت خالية عن الفصل وما يقوم مقامه كانت غنية عنه، والغني عن الشيء لا يكون معلولا له، فتحصل الحصة الموجودة في النوع من الجنس غنية عن الفصل فلا تكون الفصل فصلا، هذا خلف. والجواب: لا نسلم أن حصة النوع عين الجنس معللة بالفصل، وإن سلمناه لكن لا استحالة في استناد الأمور المتساوية إلى المؤثرات المختلفة.
The reliance of the opponents is that the differentia is a cause for the existence of the genus, and the genus is caused by it, but the cause does not seek an effect—you have known its corruption. As for their saying that a simple cause does not produce an effect, this obliges them to make the differentia composite. As for us, we do not concede that a simple cause does not produce an effect, nor do we concede that the differentia is a cause for the specific share (hissa). As for their saying that if the differentia were a cause, then every differentia would have to be constitutive (muqawwim), but that is not the case because the differentia may be divisive (muqassim) not constitutive, and that if it were a cause, it would cause a single thing by two causes—the response: as for the first, the differentia is constitutive in one respect and divisive in another; as for the second, the differentia that is the perfection of the distinguisher is only one, so there are not two causes. And as for the shaykh's saying: the differentia is a cause for the existence of the specific share, and a single personal effect does not have two independent causes—our response: the aggregate is in reality one differentia and each one is a part of it. And their also saying: the nature of the genus if it were stripped of the differentia and what stands in its place, it would be independent of it, and what is independent of something is not caused by it, so the specific share existing in the species from the genus is independent of the differentia, so the differentia would not be a differentia—this is a contradiction. The response: we do not concede that the specific share of the species is identical to the genus caused by the differentia; and if we concede it, there is no impossibility in the reliance of equivalent matters upon different causes.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the author presents objections raised by opponents (al-māni‘ūn) against the claim that the differentia (fasl) is a cause for the existence of the genus (jins), and then responds to each objection. The first objection is that if the differentia, which is a simple thing, were a cause, it would produce a composite effect (the genus), but a simple cause cannot produce a composite effect. The author responds by rejecting the premise: a simple cause can indeed produce a composite effect, so the objection fails. The second objection is that if the differentia were a cause, then every differentia would have to be constitutive (muqawwim) of the species, but in fact some differentiae are divisive (muqassim) only. The author answers that the same differentia can be constitutive in one respect and divisive in another, depending on whether it is considered relative to the lower genus or the higher genus. The third objection is that a single thing (the genus) cannot have two causes (two different differentiae). The author responds by arguing that when multiple differentiae are involved, they together form one composite differentia, so there is still only one cause. Additionally, if each part were insufficient individually, then they must be separate, but then the species would have multiple differentiae that are not simultaneous, which is not the case. The final objection is that if the genus were free of the differentia, it would be independent of it, so the differentia cannot be a cause. The author rejects the idea that the specific share of the genus in the species is identical to the genus and caused by the differentia; even if one concedes that, there is no impossibility in equivalent things (the differentia and some other factor) jointly causing the same effect. Throughout, the author maintains the traditional Ash‘ari position that the differentia is a real cause for the instantiation of the genus in the species.

Key Terms
فصل
علة
حصة
مقوم
مقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the objection that a simple cause cannot produce a composite effect?
A) He concedes and argues it is a special case.
B) He denies that the differentia is a simple cause.
C) He denies the premise and says a simple cause can produce a composite effect.
D) He changes the definition of simple cause.

Key Takeaway
The author rejects the premise that a simple cause cannot produce a composite effect, preserving the causal role of the differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify an objection from a kalam argument that a simple attribute (e.g., God's will) cannot cause multiple effects, and refute it using the author's method of denying the premise.

Objections to Fasl as Cause: Constitutive/Divisive and Independence
Author presents objections and refutations regarding fasl as cause of genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between constitutive and divisive differentiae, and refute the objection that a single effect cannot have multiple causes in different respects.

Core Idea
A differentia can be both constitutive and divisive, and a single effect can have multiple causes in different respects.

Continuing the dialectic, we now examine the latter three objections: that if fasl were a cause, all differentiae would be constitutive; a single thing would have two causes; and the genus stripped of fasl would be independent. The author provides nuanced responses that clarify the nature of differentiae and causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection about constitutive/divisive
The opponents argue: if fasl is a cause, every fasl must be constitutive (muqawwim), but some are divisive (muqassim) only. Locate this in the Arabic: 'إن الفصل لو كان علة لوجب أن يكون كل فصل مقوما'.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author says the same fasl can be constitutive in one respect (relative to lower species) and divisive in another (relative to higher genus). So it is both, but the objection fails because it assumes a false dichotomy.
3
Examine the objection about two causes
Opponents say if fasl causes the genus, then a single thing (the genus) would have two causes (if there are multiple differentiae). Find the text: 'وأنه لو كان علة لعلل الشيء الواحد بعلتين'.
4
Understand the author's refutation
The author responds that the differentia that is 'the perfection of the distinguisher' (كمال المميز) is only one. If multiple differentiae appear, they form a single composite differentia. So no two independent causes.
5
Analyze the independence objection
Opponents argue: if genus were stripped of fasl, it would be independent, so fasl cannot be a cause. The author rejects that the specific share (hissa) of the genus in the species is identical to the genus caused by fasl. Even if conceded, multiple causes can cooperate.

Absolute and Relative Property (Khassa)
Author classifies property into absolute and relative types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and relative property and correctly classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Property can be absolutely unique to a species or unique only within a certain domain.

In the five universals, khassa (property) is an accidental attribute coextensive with a species. But not all properties are equally exclusive: some belong solely to that species, while others are shared with some outsiders but still serve as distinguishing marks in a given context. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of property
Read the Arabic passage and locate the two categories: absolute (mutlaqa) and relative (idafiyya). Notice the author's phrasing 'قد تكون مطلقة... وبالإضافة'.
2
Understand the condition for absolute property
Absolute property exists only within the species and nowhere else. The condition 'لا توجد خارجة عن ذلك النوع' means it is exclusively found in that species. Example: writing and laughter for humans.
Laughter is the classic example of khassa for humans.
3
Understand the condition for relative property
Relative property exists in some other species but not all, so it becomes exclusive only within a specific comparison. The condition 'توجد في بعض المخالف للنوع دون البعض' means it is found in some others, so the property is 'خاصة لذلك النوع بالنسبة إلى عالم' - exclusive relative to that domain.
The example is the color of a jewel (substance) that does not intensify or weaken; it is a property for that substance relative to its domain, but not relative to quantities and some qualities.
4
Practice classification
Given examples from Islamic sciences, determine whether each is absolute or relative. For instance, 'being a writer' for humans is absolute; 'being a created thing' for humans is not absolute because it is shared with animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصة قد تكون مطلقة وهي التي لا توجد خارجة عن ذلك النوع كالكتابة والضحك، وبالإضافة وهي التي توجد في بعض المخالف للنوع دون البعض فتكون خاصة لذلك النوع بالنسبة إلى عالم توجد فيه كلون الجوهر غير قابل للاشتداد والضعف فإنه خاصة له لا بالنسبة إلى المقادير الكم وبعض الكيف.
The property may be absolute, which is that which does not exist outside of that species, such as writing and laughter; and relative, which is that which exists in some that differ from the species but not others, so it becomes a property for that species with respect to a domain in which it exists, like the color of the jewel that is not susceptible to intensification and weakening, for it is a property for it, not with respect to quantities (magnitudes) and some qualities.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of property (khassa). The first is absolute property, which is unique to the species and not found in any other species. Examples: writing and laughter for humans (the latter is actually the standard example for the property of man). The second is relative property, which is found in some other species but not all, so it is exclusive to the species only within a certain domain. Example: the color of a jewel (or substance) that is not subject to intensification or weakening—this is a property for that substance relative to its domain, but not relative to quantities or some qualities. The distinction is important for identifying accidental properties that are not true differentiae.

Key Terms
خاصة
خاصة مطلقة
خاصة إضافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The color of a jewel that does not intensify or weaken
B) Writing for humans
C) Not being divisible for a point
D) Being alive for animals

Key Takeaway
Property can be twofold: absolute (exclusive in all contexts) or relative (exclusive only within a specific domain).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Five Aspects of the Differentia (الفصل)
Author enumerates five aspects of differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five aspects of the differentia (al-fasl) and explain how it functions in definition.

Core Idea
The differentia is not one concept—it has five distinct aspects in predication.

In the previous lesson, you learned about the five universals. Now we dive deeper into the differentia, the essential differentiator. The author explains that the differentia can be considered from five angles, all related to how it is said of a species and how it answers the question 'what is it?' This lesson will guide you through these aspects as presented in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the differentia's role
The differentia distinguishes one species from others in the same genus. For example, 'rational' distinguishes humans from animals.
Keep in mind that the differentia is essential (ذاتي), not accidental.
2
Recognize the five aspects from the text
Even though the passage is corrupt, it mentions that the differentia is named from five aspects (خمسة أوجه) related to predication. These aspects involve how the differentia is said of the species, how it separates the species from the genus, and how it responds to the question 'what is it?'
The five aspects are standard in logic: absolute differentia, relative, etc. Refer to your textbook for the full list.
3
Connect to the question 'what is it?'
The passage states that the answer to 'what is it?' (ما هو) is the same as asking for the quiddity (ماهية). The differentia is part of that answer.
This shows the differentia's role in complete definition (حد تام).
4
Apply to a species
Take 'human' as a species. Its differentia is 'rational'. According to the five aspects, consider: (1) absolute differentia, (2) relative to genus, etc. Practice identifying each aspect.
This exercise solidifies the abstract concept.

Property (الخاصة): Absolute and Relative
Author defines property and divides it into absolute and relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and relative property and identify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Property is an accidental attribute that belongs exclusively to a species, either absolutely or relative to a genus.

After discussing the differentia, the author turns to property (الخاصة), another one of the five universals. Property is not essential like the differentia; it is an accidental attribute that is inseparable from the species. The passage defines property and divides it into absolute and relative. In this lesson, you will learn to identify and apply these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Define property from the passage
The text states that property is an accidental attribute that is not found outside the species (for absolute) or is found only in some contrary species relative to a specific genus.
Unlike the differentia, property does not answer 'what is it?' but rather 'which characteristics belong exclusively to this species?'
2
Distinguish absolute property
Absolute property is exclusive to a species in all contexts. Example from the text: writing (الكتابة) is a property of humans. It is not found in any other species.
Think of an attribute that all humans have and that no other animal has.
3
Distinguish relative property
Relative property is exclusive to a species only within a particular genus. The text gives the example of the color of a jewel that does not change intensity—it is a property for that jewel when compared to other substances, but not relative to quantity or quality categories.
This means a relative property is not exclusive absolutely, but only when contrasted with a specific group.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the property 'laughter' for humans. It is absolute? Laughter is found in some animals? Not really, but it is often considered a property. Or take 'prophethood' for Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ): it is relative because he is the final prophet compared to all prophets, but not absolute because other prophets share prophethood.
This step helps solidify the concept through real Islamic examples.

General Accident: The Accidental Predicate Across Species
Defines general accident and its properties, with a debate on predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general accident and distinguish it from a property and an essential accident.

Core Idea
General accident is an accidental predicate shared by multiple species, not exclusive or necessary.

In this section, the author defines general accident (al-'arad al-'amm) as a predicate that applies to more than one species within a genus. Unlike a property, it may be non-necessary and relative. A debated example—'white' vs 'whiteness'—clarifies how predication works. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly classifying universals in Islamic definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The author defines 'general accident' as a universal that is common to a genus, like 'existent' and 'one'. Note that it is not part of the essence.
2
Observe relativity
General accident can be relative: what is a property for a broader genus may be a general accident for a narrower species. Example: color is a property of body but a general accident for man and animal.
3
Distinguish necessary vs non-necessary
Some general accidents are necessary (like 'existent' for all substances), while others are not (like 'white' for animal). This affects their logical behavior.
4
Analyze the 'white' vs 'whiteness' debate
The author presents views: some claim general accident is 'white' (the qualified subject), others say it is 'whiteness' (the attribute). The objection from al-Mu'tar argues that 'white' means 'possessor of whiteness', so the true predicate is 'whiteness'.
This debate shows the importance of distinguishing between the attribute and the subject in logical predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومباحث العضر العام و ذلك برقاً العرض العام فذ يكون عاما للجنس كالموجود والواحد وقد يكون عرضًا عاما بالنسبة إلى النوع وإن كان خاصة بالنسبة إلى الجنس اللون خانه خاصة للجسم وعرض عام للانسان بد الحيوان وقد يكون لازمًا كالموجود والواحد وقد لا يكون كالابيض والاسود للحيوان. ب منهم من ضم خبرات هذا العرض هو العرض القيم الجوهر و هو خطأ لانه قديكون جوه وال كالا بضر و الفتحال والجوهر خارج عن ماهية العرض فيكون عضيا لة ل وقد اور دَ بَعضُ المتقد ميرغ شال العض العام البياض للانسان والشيخ انكر ذلك لان البياض للعمل على الانسان بانه هو وكال أفنان المحولات واجب الابيض كان محمول لانه يقال للانسان ايض ف العرض العام هو الأبيض البياض واعترض صاحب المعتر على فقاله المياض عن محمود فقال الابيض معناه ذو البياض ولفظة ذو النسة والمحمود الخصية هو البياض واذا كان كذلك قالا يض ليس محمد ا فقط بل هو لفظة دالة على ذات المحمول والنسبة فالمحول الحقيقة هو البياض واعلم ان هذا الحالى و كيفية اقناص الخمسة الحث إما أن تقع عن الجناسر المستثمات وفصولها أوعن اجناس الماهيات الشابة في الفسحة وفصولها والاقلغ غاية السهولة لان الانسان إذا وضع الجملة من الامور التي تصورها لإسما كان تمام العدد المشترك من تلك الأمور المنصورة هو الجنس وتمام القدر المتين هو الفصل وامت الثاني مع غاية الصعوبة لانه اذل
And the investigations of general accident (al-'arad al-'amm): that is, general accident is that which is common to the genus, such as 'existent' and 'one'. It may be a general accident relative to the species, even if it is a property relative to the genus, like color is a property of body and a general accident for man and animal. It may be necessary, like 'existent' and 'one', and may not be, like 'white' and 'black' for animal. Some have added that this accident is the accidental substance [؟], but this is a mistake, because it may be a substance like [؟] and the substance is outside the quiddity of accident, so it would be [؟]. And some predecessors have objected to the example of general accident: whiteness for man. The shaykh denied that because whiteness is an attribute on man, and [؟] the white is predicated because it is said of man. So the general accident is 'white', not 'whiteness'. The author of al-Mu'tar [؟] objected to his saying that whiteness is not predicated, saying: 'white' means 'possessor of whiteness', and the word 'possessor' indicates relationship, and the predicated attribute is whiteness. If so, then 'white' is not a predicate only, but rather a word indicating the essence of the predicate and the relationship, so the true predicate is whiteness. Know that this [؟] and the method of acquiring the five universals is either by real genera and their differentiae, or by genera of quiddities that are similar in scope and their differentiae. The first is extremely easy because if a person sets up the sum of the things he imagines as a name, the total number of common things among those imagined is the genus, and the total of the specific [؟] is the differentia. The second is extremely difficult because [؟].
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage covers two main topics: general accident (العرض العام) and the method of acquiring the five universals. First, the author defines general accident as a predicate that applies to multiple species within a genus, e.g., 'existent' and 'one'. It can be relative: what is a property for a lower genus may be a general accident for a higher one (like color for body vs. animal). Then the author discusses a controversy: some claim that the general accident includes substances, but this is rejected. The example of 'whiteness' for man is debated: the general accident should be 'white' (the thing qualified) not 'whiteness' (the attribute), because predicates are said of subjects. However, the author of al-Mu'tar objects that 'white' implies 'possessor of whiteness', so the real predicate is 'whiteness'. The author does not settle the debate here. Finally, the passage introduces a method for grasping the five universals: either through real genera (like animal as genus for man) or through conceptual genera (like quiddities that share a common scope). The first method is easy: simply list the common attributes to find the genus, and the special attribute to find the differentia. The second method is very difficult, but the text cuts off. This lesson thus completes the discussion of the predicables and provides a practical approach to defining species.

Key Terms
العرض العام
العرض الخاص

C) There is no difference; they are synonyms.
D) 'Whiteness' is a property, 'white' is a general accident.

Key Takeaway
General accident is a universal accidental predicate that applies across multiple species within a genus, distinguished from properties by its lack of exclusivity.

Method of Acquiring the Five Universals from Real and Conceptual Genera
Introduces two methods of extracting the five universals from real or conceptual genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two methods of extracting genus and differentia from a given concept using real or conceptual genera.

Core Idea
The five universals are extracted either from real genera (easy) or from conceptual genera (difficult) by comparing common and specific attributes.

After defining the universals, the author presents a practical method for 'iqnāṣ' (acquiring/hunting) the five universals. The method has two variants: one dealing with real genera (like animal for man) and the other with conceptual genera (quiddities similar in scope). The first is straightforward: list common attributes to find the genus, and the specific attribute to find the differentia. The second is much harder and requires deeper conceptual analysis. This method is essential for constructing accurate definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two approaches
Real genera are natural classes (e.g., animal, plant). Conceptual genera are mental constructs (e.g., categories like 'body' or 'substance'). The method works for both.
2
Apply the easy method (real genera)
Take a species like 'man'. Imagine a set of individuals. The common attributes shared by all are the genus (e.g., animal). The specific attribute unique to man is the differentia (e.g., rational).
This is straightforward because real genera have clear commonalities.
3
Apply the difficult method (conceptual genera)
For abstract quiddities like 'possible' or 'necessary', find a set of similar concepts. Their common aspect is the genus, and the distinguishing aspect is the differentia. This requires careful conceptual analysis.
The passage notes this is 'extremely difficult'.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Take the concept of 'wudu' in fiqh. Compare different instances (wudu for prayer, wudu for tawaf). The common aspects (washing specific limbs) form the genus, and the specific intention (niyyah) or order might be the differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومباحث العضر العام و ذلك برقاً العرض العام فذ يكون عاما للجنس كالموجود والواحد وقد يكون عرضًا عاما بالنسبة إلى النوع وإن كان خاصة بالنسبة إلى الجنس اللون خانه خاصة للجسم وعرض عام للانسان بد الحيوان وقد يكون لازمًا كالموجود والواحد وقد لا يكون كالابيض والاسود للحيوان. ب منهم من ضم خبرات هذا العرض هو العرض القيم الجوهر و هو خطأ لانه قديكون جوه وال كالا بضر و الفتحال والجوهر خارج عن ماهية العرض فيكون عضيا لة ل وقد اور دَ بَعضُ المتقد ميرغ شال العض العام البياض للانسان والشيخ انكر ذلك لان البياض للعمل على الانسان بانه هو وكال أفنان المحولات واجب الابيض كان محمول لانه يقال للانسان ايض ف العرض العام هو الأبيض البياض واعترض صاحب المعتر على فقاله المياض عن محمود فقال الابيض معناه ذو البياض ولفظة ذو النسة والمحمود الخصية هو البياض واذا كان كذلك قالا يض ليس محمد ا فقط بل هو لفظة دالة على ذات المحمول والنسبة فالمحول الحقيقة هو البياض واعلم ان هذا الحالى و كيفية اقناص الخمسة الحث إما أن تقع عن الجناسر المستثمات وفصولها أوعن اجناس الماهيات الشابة في الفسحة وفصولها والاقلغ غاية السهولة لان الانسان إذا وضع الجملة من الامور التي تصورها لإسما كان تمام العدد المشترك من تلك الأمور المنصورة هو الجنس وتمام القدر المتين هو الفصل وامت الثاني مع غاية الصعوبة لانه اذل
And the investigations of general accident (al-'arad al-'amm): that is, general accident is that which is common to the genus, such as 'existent' and 'one'. It may be a general accident relative to the species, even if it is a property relative to the genus, like color is a property of body and a general accident for man and animal. It may be necessary, like 'existent' and 'one', and may not be, like 'white' and 'black' for animal. Some have added that this accident is the accidental substance [؟], but this is a mistake, because it may be a substance like [؟] and the substance is outside the quiddity of accident, so it would be [؟]. And some predecessors have objected to the example of general accident: whiteness for man. The shaykh denied that because whiteness is an attribute on man, and [؟] the white is predicated because it is said of man. So the general accident is 'white', not 'whiteness'. The author of al-Mu'tar [؟] objected to his saying that whiteness is not predicated, saying: 'white' means 'possessor of whiteness', and the word 'possessor' indicates relationship, and the predicated attribute is whiteness. If so, then 'white' is not a predicate only, but rather a word indicating the essence of the predicate and the relationship, so the true predicate is whiteness. Know that this [؟] and the method of acquiring the five universals is either by real genera and their differentiae, or by genera of quiddities that are similar in scope and their differentiae. The first is extremely easy because if a person sets up the sum of the things he imagines as a name, the total number of common things among those imagined is the genus, and the total of the specific [؟] is the differentia. The second is extremely difficult because [؟].
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage covers two main topics: general accident (العرض العام) and the method of acquiring the five universals. First, the author defines general accident as a predicate that applies to multiple species within a genus, e.g., 'existent' and 'one'. It can be relative: what is a property for a lower genus may be a general accident for a higher one (like color for body vs. animal). Then the author discusses a controversy: some claim that the general accident includes substances, but this is rejected. The example of 'whiteness' for man is debated: the general accident should be 'white' (the thing qualified) not 'whiteness' (the attribute), because predicates are said of subjects. However, the author of al-Mu'tar objects that 'white' implies 'possessor of whiteness', so the real predicate is 'whiteness'. The author does not settle the debate here. Finally, the passage introduces a method for grasping the five universals: either through real genera (like animal as genus for man) or through conceptual genera (like quiddities that share a common scope). The first method is easy: simply list the common attributes to find the genus, and the special attribute to find the differentia. The second method is very difficult, but the text cuts off. This lesson thus completes the discussion of the predicables and provides a practical approach to defining species.

Key Terms

الجنس الحقيقي
الجنس الذهني

Key Takeaway
Acquiring the five universals is a practical skill: for real genera, simply compare instances to find common and specific attributes; for conceptual genera, the task is more abstract and challenging.

Types of Property and Common Accident in Logic
Defines khassa and 'ard 'amm with their subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify properties (khassa) and common accidents ('ard 'amm) into their subtypes and identify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Properties are specific to one species; common accidents span multiple species.

The five universals include two kinds of accidents: property and common accident. This lesson clarifies their subdivisions, a key distinction in logical definitions used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Locate the definitions of khassa and 'ard 'amm in the passage. Notice that khassa is specific to one species, while 'ard 'amm applies to multiple species.
2
Distinguish subtypes of khassa
The passage mentions that khassa may be commensurate (musawiya) like 'capacity to laugh' for man, or more specific (akhas) like 'actually laughing'. It may be inseparable or separable. Identify these in the Arabic.
Commensurate means it covers exactly the entire species.
3
Distinguish subtypes of 'ard 'amm
The passage describes 'ard 'amm as general to the genus (like 'existent') or general to the species while being a property of a higher genus (like 'color' for man). It may be necessary (like 'existent') or non-necessary (like 'white' for animal).
Examples: 'existent' is necessary for all substances; 'white' is non-necessary for a body.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Think of examples from usul al-fiqh or kalam for each subtype. For instance, 'intoxication' is a khassa for wine? Actually, it's a common accident among intoxicants. Use the criteria from the passage.
In analogical reasoning, misidentifying a common accident as a property can invalidate a qiyas.

A property relative to certain particulars is a property for the lowest species, the intermediate species, and the highest species and the highest genus, because its being a property for them has nothing but that [commonality]. Whether that which is particular to the type is a property, it may be commensurate like 'capacity to laugh' for man, and it may be more specific like 'actually laughing'. Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of two kinds of accidents in logic: al-khassa (the property) and al-'ard al-'amm (the common accident). The author first describes khassa: it can be commensurate (musawiya), like the capacity to laugh for man, or more specific (akhas), like actually laughing. It may be inseparable (lazim) or separable (mufariq). The commentator al-Mu'tar (likely from a commentary) then retorts that 'white' means 'possessor of whiteness', and the actual predicate is whiteness itself. This shows the precision required in logical predication.

The Predicate Debate: Is 'White' or 'Whiteness' the Common Accident?
Records a debate about the example 'white' for man.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the debate about whether 'white' or 'whiteness' is the correct common accident for man, and evaluate the arguments using the rules of logical predication.

Core Idea
Logical predication requires distinguishing the accident itself from the subject described.

Some earlier logicians gave 'whiteness' as an example of a common accident for man. A shaykh objected, saying 'white' is predicated, not 'whiteness'. The commentator al-Mu'tar responded that 'white' implies a relation, so 'whiteness' is the actual predicate. This debate sharpens our understanding of predication in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial example
Some predecessors gave 'whiteness' (al-bayad) as a common accident for man. Locate this in the Arabic: 'وقد ورد بعض المتقدمين مثال العرض العام البياض للإنسان'.
2
The shaykh's objection
The shaykh denied this because 'whiteness' is not predicated of man; rather 'white' (al-abyad) is predicated, as in 'Zayd is white'. Identify the shaykh's reasoning: 'البياض لا يعقل على الإنسان'.
This resembles the logical rule that the predicate is an adjective, not an abstract noun.
3
Al-Mu'tar's counter-objection
Sahib al-Mu'tar argued that 'white' means 'possessor of whiteness' (dhu bayad), and 'possessor' adds a relation. Thus the actual predicate is 'whiteness' itself. Find his statement: 'الأبيض معناه ذو البياض'.
This shows the complexity of connotative terms in predication.
4
Evaluate the logical issue
Which position is correct according to standard Aristotelian logic? Consider whether the predicate in 'Zayd is white' is 'white' (an adjective) or 'whiteness' (a universal). The debate hinges on whether we consider the accident in itself or as instantiated.
In Islamic logic, both positions have support; the passage leaves it unresolved.

Two Scenarios for Capturing the Five Universals
Explains the difficulty and method of using division to capture the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two scenarios for capturing the five universals and explain why division is necessary for the difficult scenario.

Core Idea
Division is the key when genera and differentiae aren't pre-known.

The author begins by noting that capturing the five universals can be either extremely easy or extremely difficult depending on whether the genera and differentiae are already known. When they are known, one simply lists the common and distinguishing elements. When dealing with a new existent, however, we must first understand its essence and attributes, making the task far harder. Division is introduced as the reliable method for this difficult case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the opening lines of the passage. The author says capturing the five universals either occurs from 'complete genera and their differentiae' or from 'genera of simple essences in extension and their differentiae'. These are the two scenarios.
The first scenario is easy; the second is extremely difficult.
2
Understand why the second is difficult
When a particular existent is perceived, we know in sum that there is a self-subsistent essence and attributes. But to capture the universals, we need to know what the essence is, what the attributes are, and which are common and which differentiate. This requires detailed analysis.
The author stresses that this makes verification extremely hard.
3
See the need for division
Since the second scenario is so difficult, the author proposes division (al-qisma) as a reliable method. This is the core tool for reducing compounds to simples to identify the generic and specific parts.
Division is the path to clarity when direct knowledge is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا الحال و كيفية اقناص الخمسة الحث إما أن تقع عن الجناس المستثمات وفصولها أو عن اجناس الماهيات الشابة في الفسحة وفصولها والأول غاية السهولة لان الإنسان إذا وضع الجملة من الأمور التي تصورها لاسم كان تمام العدد المشترك من تلك الأمور المنصورة هو الجنس وتمام القدر المتين هو الفصل والثاني مع غاية الصعوبة. ثم وقع بصر على موجود معين علمنا الجملة أن هناك ذاتا قائمة بنفسها. علمنا أن هناك صفات قائمة بتلك الذات فأما الذات أن نعلم أن الذات ايش هي والصفات ايش هي ولكم هي ومن يصعب علينا معرفة ذلك. ثم إذا عرفنا سكين يشتركان من بعض الوجوه وبيانات من وجه آخر فلا تمكننا أن نعرف أن تمام العدد المشترك الشهو وكيف هو وأن تمام العدد الحمين أين هو وكيف هو وإذا كان ذلك صعبا عر فكان اقناص الفصول والأجناس على سبيل التحقيق غاية العسر. لكن من الطرق المعتبرة فيه القسمة وهي تنقسم الى إما إلى اجزائه وإلى قسمة الكلى الى جزئياته. أما الأول فمعناه حصول الكل من الأجزاء إما أن يكون ذهنيا كتركب السواد من جنسه الذي هو الكون وفصله الذي هو قابضية البصر مثلا ففي هذا التركيب عسر خاص ان الوقوع أصلًا على ما ستعرفه وإما أن يكون خارجيا لما طبيعيا كتركب المدن عن الأعضاء أو صناعيا كتركب السرير وكل واحد منهما قد يكون تركيبًا كالأعضاء من الأخلاط والسرير من الخشب والحديد وقد يكون طبيعيا كالنار. وأما الثاني فالحلي الذي يكون مورد القسمة إما الجنس أو النوع أو الفصل أو الشخص وعليك بالتفصيل. وبالجملة فالقسمة طريق إلى تقليد المركبات إلى بسائطها وحتى حصلت التساقط لمتن الجزء الجنسي عن الفصلي أو يكون ذلك العسر في المشاركات لاش هذه المحنة. فلنذكر الثانيات اولا وهي كة فا مشاركة الجنس مع الفقرة كونها جودى الماهية ويتفرع عليه الخواص الأربع التي للجزرب في أن کل واحد منها من حيث هو جنس وفصل مقول عن طريق ما هو بالنسبة إلى النوع أو في أن كل واحد منهما غير مقصود إليه بالذات والألما أو الوصف الخارجي عن الماهية وقت الا واحد منها إلا أن تقسم إلى الجنس و التوسع فيح.
Know that this situation and the method of capturing the five universals either occurs from complete genera and their differentiae or from genera of simple essences in extension and their differentiae. The first is extremely easy because if a man posits the sum of things that he conceptualizes for a name, the complete common number of those sustained things is the genus, and the complete determined measure is the differentia. The second is extremely difficult. Then if a glance falls on a particular existent, we know in sum that there is a self-subsistent essence. We know that there are attributes subsisting through that essence. As for the essence, it is difficult for us to know what the essence is, and what the attributes are, and how many they are, and that. Then if we know that they share in some respects and differ in others, it is not possible for us to know what the complete common number is and how it is, and what the complete differentiating number is and where it is and how it is. And if that is difficult, then capturing differentiae and genera by way of verification is extremely hard. But among the reliable methods in this is division, and it is divided into division into parts and division of the universal into its particulars. As for the first, its meaning is the whole's existence from the parts; it may be mental like the composition of blackness from its genus which is color and its differentia which is, for example, the covering of sight; this composition has a special difficulty in actual occurrence as you will learn. Or it may be external, either natural like the composition of the body from organs or artificial like the composition of the bed. Each of them may be a synthesis like the organs from humors and the bed from wood and iron, or it may be natural like fire. As for the second, it is that whose subject of division is either genus or species or differentia or individual, and you must elaborate. In summary, division is a way to reduce compounds to simples until the separation of the generic part from the specific part occurs, or that difficulty lies in the commonalities despite this effort. So let us mention the secondaries first, and it is that the genus's sharing with the differentia is in being a part of the essence, and from this branch the four properties that belong to each of them: that each of them, as genus and differentia, is predicated in the 'what is it?' way relative to the species; and that each of them is not intended by itself and the pain or the description external to the essence at the time... except that it is divided into genus and extension.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage discusses the method of capturing (iqtanas) the five universals: genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident. The author distinguishes two scenarios: (1) when the complete genera and differentiae are known, it is easy—one simply lists the common elements (genus) and the distinguishing one (differentia). (2) When dealing with simple essences (like a new existent), it is extremely difficult because we first perceive a self-subsistent essence, then we want to know its essence and attributes, and then we need to identify what is common and what differentiates it from others. Since this is hard, the reliable method is division (al-qisma). Division is of two types: (a) division of the whole into its parts (tajzi'a) which can be mental (like defining blackness via its genus 'color' and differentia 'light-absorbing') or external (natural like body parts or artificial like a bed); (b) division of the universal into its particulars (such as dividing genus into species, or species into individuals). The author notes that division ultimately reduces compounds to simples until the generic part separates from the specific. He then begins to discuss the four properties of genus and differentia (e.g., both are predicated 'what is it?' of the species, neither is intended by itself, etc.), but the text is cut off.

Types of Division: Whole into Parts and Universal into Particulars
Explains the difficulty and method of using division to capture the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of division (whole into parts and universal into particulars) and give examples of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Division unravels compounds into simples, whether by splitting a whole or a universal.

After establishing that division is essential for capturing universals in difficult cases, the author distinguishes two fundamental types: dividing a whole into its parts (tajzi'a) and dividing a universal into its particulars (qismat al-kulli ila juz'iyyatih). Each type has subcategories and applications, from mental analysis to physical decomposition. Understanding these types is crucial for applying division effectively in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two main types of division
The text clearly states: division is divided into 'division into parts' and 'division of the universal into its particulars'. The first deals with wholes and their components; the second deals with universals and their instances.
These are distinct logical operations.
2
Analyze the first type: whole into parts
The author gives examples: mental composition (e.g., blackness composed of genus 'color' and differentia 'light-absorbing'), external natural (e.g., body composed of organs), and external artificial (e.g., bed composed of wood and iron). Note that each may be a synthesis of elements.
Mental composition is about conceptual parts; external is about physical parts.
3
Analyze the second type: universal into particulars
The subject of division here can be genus, species, differentia, or individual. For example, dividing the genus 'animal' into its species 'human' and 'horse', or dividing the species 'human' into individual persons. This type helps clarify the scope of a universal.
This is crucial for definition and classification.
4
Understand the goal of division
The author says division is a way to reduce compounds to simples until the generic part separates from the specific part. This reveals the essential structure of a concept.
The ultimate aim is to identify the five universals clearly.

واعلم أن هذا الحال و كيفية اقناص الخمسة الحث إما أن تقع عن الجناس المستثمات وفصولها أو عن اجناس الماهيات الشابة في الفسحة وفصولها والأول غاية السهولة لان الإنسان إذا وضع الجملة من الأمور التي تصورها لاسم كان تمام العدد المشترك من تلك الأمور المنصورة هو الجنس وتمام القدر المتين هو الفصل والثاني مع غاية الصعوبة. ثم وقع بصر على موجود معين علمنا الجملة أن هناك ذاتا قائمة بنفسها. علمنا أن هناك صفات قائمة بتلك الذات فأما الذات أن نعلم أن الذات ايش هي والصفات ايش هي ولكم هي ومن يصعب علينا معرفة ذلك. ثم إذا عرفنا سكين يشتركان من بعض الوجوه وبيانات من وجه آخر فلا تمكننا أن نعرف أن تمام العدد المشترك الشهو وكيف هو وأن تمام العدد الحمين أين هو وكيف هو وإذا كان ذلك صعبا عر فكان اقناص الفصول والأجناس على سبيل التحقيق غاية العسر. لكن من الطرق المعتبرة فيه القسمة وهي تنقسم الى إما إلى اجزائه وإلى قسمة الكلى الى جزئياته. أما الأول فمعناه حصول الكل من الأجزاء إما أن يكون ذهنيا كتركب السواد من جنسه الذي هو الكون وفصله الذي هو قابضية البصر مثلا ففي هذا التركيب عسر خاص ان الوقوع أصلًا على ما ستعرفه وإما أن يكون خارجيا لما طبيعيا كتركب المدن عن الأعضاء أو صناعيا كتركب السرير وكل واحد منهما قد يكون تركيبًا كالأعضاء من الأخلاط والسرير من الخشب والحديد وقد يكون طبيعيا كالنار. وأما الثاني فالحلي الذي يكون مورد القسمة إما الجنس أو النوع أو الفصل أو الشخص وعليك بالتفصيل. وبالجملة فالقسمة طريق إلى تقليد المركبات إلى بسائطها وحتى حصلت التساقط لمتن الجزء الجنسي عن الفصلي أو يكون ذلك العسر في المشاركات لاش هذه المحنة. فلنذكر الثانيات اولا وهي كة فا مشاركة الجنس مع الفقرة كونها جودى الماهية ويتفرع عليه الخواص الأربع التي للجزرب في أن کل واحد منها من حيث هو جنس وفصل مقول عن طريق ما هو بالنسبة إلى النوع أو في أن كل واحد منهما غير مقصود إليه بالذات والألما أو الوصف الخارجي عن الماهية وقت الا واحد منها إلا أن تقسم إلى الجنس و التوسع فيح.
Know that this situation and the method of capturing the five universals either occurs from complete genera and their differentiae or from genera of simple essences in extension and their differentiae. The first is extremely easy because if a man posits the sum of things that he conceptualizes for a name, the complete common number of those sustained things is the genus, and the complete determined measure is the differentia. The second is extremely difficult. Then if a glance falls on a particular existent, we know in sum that there is a self-subsistent essence. We know that there are attributes subsisting through that essence. As for the essence, it is difficult for us to know what the essence is, and what the attributes are, and how many they are, and that. Then if we know that they share in some respects and differ in others, it is not possible for us to know what the complete common number is and how it is, and what the complete differentiating number is and where it is and how it is. And if that is difficult, then capturing differentiae and genera by way of verification is extremely hard. But among the reliable methods in this is division, and it is divided into division into parts and division of the universal into its particulars. As for the first, its meaning is the whole's existence from the parts; it may be mental like the composition of blackness from its genus which is color and its differentia which is, for example, the covering of sight; this composition has a special difficulty in actual occurrence as you will learn. Or it may be external, either natural like the composition of the body from organs or artificial like the composition of the bed. Each of them may be a synthesis like the organs from humors and the bed from wood and iron, or it may be natural like fire. As for the second, it is that whose subject of division is either genus or species or differentia or individual, and you must elaborate. In summary, division is a way to reduce compounds to simples until the separation of the generic part from the specific part occurs, or that difficulty lies in the commonalities despite this effort. So let us mention the secondaries first, and it is that the genus's sharing with the differentia is in being a part of the essence, and from this branch the four properties that belong to each of them: that each of them, as genus and differentia, is predicated in the 'what is it?' way relative to the species; and that each of them is not intended by itself and the pain or the description external to the essence at the time... except that it is divided into genus and extension.

Genus and Differentia: Essential Constituents
Author enumerates commonalities between genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four common properties shared by genus and differentia as essential constituents of a quiddity.

Core Idea
Genus and differentia are both essential, but neither is the goal; the species is.

The five universals are related in specific ways, and the most fundamental relation is between genus and differentia. Both are essential (wujudi) to the quiddity, which gives rise to four derivative properties. Understanding these commonalities helps you construct correct definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Locate 'genus' and 'differentia' in the passage. Recognize that they are both essential constituents of the quiddity.
2
Extract the shared property
The text states they share being 'wujudi al-mahiyya' (essential to the quiddity). This is the foundational commonality.
3
Derive the four branch properties
From that commonality, four properties follow: each answers 'what is it?', each is predicated of the species in the same manner, each as genus/differentia is predicated of the species, and each is not intended by itself but the species is intended.
4
Apply to a juristic definition
Consider the definition of 'prayer (salat)': genus = 'act of worship', differentia = 'with specific movements and recitations'. Both are essential, but the intended entity is the composite species 'prayer'.
This shows how the commonalities work in a real Islamic science example.

Let us first mention the subsequent matters: the commonality between genus and differentia in both being essential to the quiddity, from which branch the four properties: each is said in answer to 'what is it?' or in the manner of 'what is it?'; each is predicated of the species composed of them in the manner of 'what is it?'; each as genus and differentia is predicated in the manner of 'what is it?' with respect to the species; each is not intended by itself but the species is intended. The third is the property and the external [accident]... one of them except that it is divided into genus and expansion so the species results. The third remaining is such that it is intended with the primary intention and with expansion in being said in answer to 'what is it?', even if one of them is by participation. The second is the specificity of property with property: that the complete [definition] does not lack them...

Commentary
This passage enumerates the commonalities (musharakat) between each pair of the five universals: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw'), property (khassa), and accident ('arad). The text begins with the commonality between genus and differentia: both are positive constituents of the quiddity (wujudi al-mahiyya). From this, four properties follow: (1) each is said in answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa); (2) each is predicated of the species in the same manner; (3) each as such is predicated of the species; (4) neither is intended by itself—rather the species is intended. The passage then appears to discuss the commonality of property with the others, but the text is too garbled to parse clearly. The author likely continues to list the commonalities between differentia and species, species and property, etc., noting conditions such as being inside or outside the quiddity, permanence, and complete definition. For example, in fiqh, the genus 'act of worship' and differentia 'specific intention' are both essential, while the property 'requires ablution' is external but inseparable, and the accident 'performed in mosque' is external and separable. The commentary would normally explain each commonality, but due to OCR corruption, a precise explanation is not possible here.

Property and Accident: External but Distinct Relations
Author discusses commonalities between property and accident and their relations with other universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish property from accident based on their relation to the quiddity and their role in definition.

Core Idea
Both property and accident are external to the quiddity, but property is inseparable while accident is separable.

Beyond genus and differentia, there are property (khassa) and accident ('arad). Both lie outside the essence, but they differ in permanence and their usefulness for complete definitions. Understanding these commonalities is crucial for constructing accurate descriptions (rusum).

Guided Path
1
Identify property and accident
Locate references to 'khassa' (property) and 'arad' (accident) in the passage. Note that both are described as 'external' to the quiddity.
2
Recognize the commonality
The text indicates that both property and accident are 'kharija 'an al-mahiyya' (outside the essence). This is their shared feature.
3
Note the difference in permanence
Property is necessary for the species (lāzim), while accident is contingent and can separate. The passage hints at this by discussing 'duwam' (permanence) and 'tamam' (completeness).
Property can be used in a complete description (rasm tāmm), while accident only in an incomplete one.
4
Apply to fiqh example
In the definition of 'salawat' (prayer): 'acts of worship with specific intentions'. The property is 'requires purity' (inseparable), while the accident is 'performed in congregation' (separable).
This helps in distinguishing essential from non-essential when deriving rulings.

For example, in fiqh, the genus 'act of worship' and differentia 'specific intention' are both essential, while the property 'requires ablution' is external but inseparable, and the accident 'performed in mosque' is external and separable. The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Mutual Relations of the Five Universals
Author enumerates the mutual relations between genus, species, differentia, property, and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential from accidental universals and explain how each pair among the five universals participates in predication and definition.

Core Idea
The five universals form a structured set: three essential and two accidental, each with specific roles in definition and description.

In this lesson, we explore how the five universals relate to each other. The classical logicians analyzed these relations to show which universals can combine to form complete definitions (hadd) versus descriptions (rasm). Understanding these mutual participations (musharakat) is crucial for constructing precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five universals
Recall the definitions of the five universals: genus (jins), species (naw'), differentia (fasl), property (khassa), and accident ('arad). Write down their standard descriptions based on what you have learned.
Genus and species answer 'what is it?'; differentia answers 'which thing in its essence?'; property and accident are non-essential.
2
Classify as essential or accidental
Determine which universals are essential (dhati) – i.e., part of the quiddity – and which are accidental ('aradi). Genus, species, and differentia are essential; property and accident are accidental.
3
Examine pair-wise relations
For each pair of universals, consider how they share or differ in predication. For example, genus and species both answer 'what is it?', but genus is broader; differentia and species are both co-extensive but answer different questions; property and accident are both outside the quiddity.
Some pairs cannot exist together in a definition (e.g., genus and species are exclusive as co-predicables).
4
Identify which pairs form definitions or descriptions
Note that genus + differentia form a complete definition (hadd haqiqi); genus + property or differentia + property form a complete description (rasm tamm); other combinations may be partial.
Property and accident alone cannot define the essence.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Choose a concept like 'salah' (prayer) or 'iman' (faith) and try to identify its five universals. Then check which pairs among them participate in predication as described.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the mutual relations (musharakat) among the five universals: genus (jins), species (naw'), differentia (fasl), property (khassa), and accident ('arad). The author, likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, explains how each universal shares with others in terms of predication, essentiality, and constancy. The text is highly corrupt, but typical themes include: genus and species share in being predicated in answer to 'what is it?' but differ in scope; differentia is co-extensive with species but not broader; property and accident are external to the quiddity yet may be permanent. The discussion proceeds pair by pair, noting which participations are valid and which are not. For example, the differentia participates with the species in being an essential part, but differs in that the differentia is not predicated of the species in answer to 'what is it?'. The passage also notes that some universals (like genus and species) can be predicated by 'general possibility' (imkan 'amm) of many things, while others are restricted. The enumeration concludes with a note that among the five universals, each description (wasf) involves four sharing in something and the fifth being unique—this likely refers to the fact that four of the universals are essential (dhati) and one is accidental, or a similar classification. Due to OCR corruption, the exact details are unclear, but the overall structure is recognizable as a standard exposition of the interrelations of the five universals in classical Islamic logic.

Three Layers of Modalities and Their Interrelations
Author enumerates three layers of modalities and explains their logical relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modal layers (necessity, impossibility, specific possibility), list their components and negations, and explain why across-layer negations do not correlate.

Core Idea
Modal modalities form a trichotomy: necessity, impossibility, and specific possibility; their negations reveal general possibility.

This passage systematically classifies the three fundamental modal categories (jihāt) and their negations, showing how they interrelate. Understanding these layers is essential for modal reasoning in Islamic demonstration (burhān), especially when distinguishing necessary, impossible, and contingent existence in Kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modal layers
The author explicitly states there are three layers (طبقات ثلاث). Find the Arabic names: الوجوب (necessity), الإمتناع (impossibility), and الممکن الخاص (specific possibility).
These correspond to the three basic modalities in Islamic logic.
2
List the components of the first layer (necessity)
Under necessity, the author lists: واجب أن يوجد (necessary to exist), ممتنع أن لا يوجد (impossible not to exist), and ليس ممتنع أن لا يوجد (not impossible not to exist), the last being general possibility of non-existence.
Note that 'not impossible not to exist' is not equivalent to specific possibility.
3
List the components of the second layer (impossibility)
Under impossibility: واجب أن لا يوجد (necessary not to exist), ممتنع أن يوجد (impossible to exist), and ليس ممتنع أن يوجد (not impossible to exist), i.e., general possibility of existence.
Parallel structure to the first layer, with existence swapped for non-existence.
4
List the components of the third layer (specific possibility)
Specific possibility: ممکن أن يوجد ليس ممتنعا أن يوجد (possible to exist, not impossible to exist) and ممکن أن لا يوجد ليس ممتنعا أن لا يوجد (possible not to exist, not impossible not to exist).
This layer expresses genuine contingency: neither necessary nor impossible.
5
Understand the interrelation principle
The author states that the modalities are necessarily correlative and converse (متلازمة متعاكسة), and their negations similarly. However, negations of one layer do not correlate with another layer because under the negation of any one modality fall the other two.
For example, 'not necessary' (لا وجوب) does not imply impossibility; it leaves open both impossibility and specific possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بحثنا عن ماهيات هذه الجهات فلنكلم في كيفية تلازمها، وهي طبقات ثلاث: الطبقة الأولى: الوجوب ونقائضها: واجب أن يوجد، ممتنع أن لا يوجد، ليس ممتنع أن لا يوجد، وهو الممكن العام أن لا يوجد. الطبقة الثانية: الإمتناع ونقائضها: واجب أن لا يوجد، ممتنع أن يوجد، ليس ممتنع أن يوجد، وهو الممكن العام أن يوجد. الطبقة الثالثة: الممكن الخاص ونقائضها: ممكن أن يوجد ليس ممتنعا أن يوجد، ممكن أن لا يوجد ليس ممتنعا أن لا يوجد. ثم هذه الجهات متلازمة متعاكسة، فنقائضها متلازمة متعاكسة، ونقائض كل طبقة لا تلازم الطبقة الأخرى، لأن الجهات لما كانت ثلاثا اندرج تحت نقيض كل واحدة منها الباقيتان.
We have investigated the essences of these modalities, so let us speak about how they are connected. They are three layers: The first layer: necessity and its negations: necessary to exist, impossible to not exist, not impossible to not exist, which is general possibility of non-existence. The second layer: impossibility and its negations: necessary to not exist, impossible to exist, not impossible to exist, which is general possibility of existence. The third layer: specific possibility and its negations: possible to exist not impossible to exist, possible to not exist not impossible to not exist. Then these modalities are necessarily correlative and converse, so their negations are likewise correlative and converse, and the negations of one layer are not correlative with another layer because since the modalities are three, under the negation of each one fall the remaining two.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage classifies the three fundamental modal categories (jihāt) and their negations, showing how they interrelate. The first layer is necessity (wujūb) which includes: 'necessary to exist' (wājib an yūjad), 'impossible to not exist' (mumtani‘ an lā yūjad), and 'not impossible to not exist' which is equivalent to 'general possibility of non-existence' (al-mumkin al-‘āmm an lā yūjad). The second layer is impossibility (imtinā‘) with analogous negations leading to general possibility of existence. The third layer is specific possibility (al-mumkin al-khāṣṣ) which is the balanced possibility of both existence and non-existence, with its own negations. The author then notes that these modalities are interconnected (mutalāzima muta‘ākisa) meaning that from one you can infer its correlates, and their negations also correlate. However, negations across layers do not correlate because negation of one modality implies the disjunction of the other two. For example, the negation of necessity (lā wujūb) does not imply impossibility alone but leaves open possibility. This classification is crucial for modal logic in demonstration (burhān).

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujūb): The first modal layer: necessity of existence, including its negations and correlates. [Note: In later logic, wujūb is often treated as one of the three jihāt; here it is a layer with submodalities.]
الإمتناع (al-imtinā‘): The second modal layer: impossibility of existence, with its negations leading to general possibility of existence.
الممكن الخاص (al-mumkin al-khāṣṣ): The third modal layer: specific possibility, meaning neither necessary nor impossible, i.e., contingent. [Note: Sometimes called mumkin khāṣṣ in contrast to mumkin ‘āmm which is simply negation of impossibility.]
الممكن العام (al-mumkin al-‘āmm): General possibility: the negation of impossibility, broader than specific possibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third layer (specific possibility), which of the following is true?
A) It is necessary that it exists.
B) It is impossible that it does not exist.
C) It is possible to exist and possible not to exist, without necessity.
D) It is impossible to exist.

Key Takeaway
The three modal layers (necessity, impossibility, specific possibility) and their negations form a complete classification; each layer's negation covers the other two modalities, so across-layer negations do not correlate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the three layers, classify the following states: (a) Allah exists necessarily; (b) a partner with Allah exists impossibly; (c) a human being exists possibly. For each, identify which layer and which specific expression (e.g., wājib an yūjad) it corresponds to.
?
Explain why the negation of necessity (lā wujūb) does not imply impossibility but leaves open possibility. Provide a concrete example from Islamic theology (e.g., the existence of the world before its creation is not necessary, but it is possible, not impossible).

Causality and Knowledge: Why the Effect Only Gives Universal Knowledge
Author argues that knowledge of the effect does not yield knowledge of the specific cause, only the absolute cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between knowledge of the absolute cause and knowledge of the specific cause, and evaluate the objection that knowledge of the cause yields knowledge of the specific effect.

Core Idea
Knowledge of an effect reveals the universal cause, not the particular.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical exchange about what knowledge of an effect can tell us about its cause. An opponent argues that if we know the cause, we know the specific effect. The author refines this claim: knowledge of the cause gives only universal knowledge of the effect. This distinction is crucial for understanding how we reason about causality in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author's own position: knowledge of the effect gives knowledge of the absolute cause, not the specific cause. Find the sentence that states this.
Look for 'العلم بالمعلول العلم بالعلة المطلقة لا العلم بالعلة المعتمدية'
2
Find the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that the contingent, in itself, requires knowledge of its specific cause. Locate the objection in the passage.
The objection likely begins with 'القائل أن يقول'
3
Analyze the author's response
The author uses the concept of universality: the effect known through its cause is universal (كلى). Even if the cause is specific, the knowledge remains universal because 'الكلى المقيد بالكلى كلى'.
4
Apply to the example of the Necessary Existent
The author extends the argument: knowledge of the Necessary Existent (واجب الوجود) by essence gives knowledge of individuals only as temporal, not as universal. This refutes the opponent's claim.
This shows the practical implication for Kalam.

The specific effect is not from the specific cause, but from the absolute cause. For it follows that from knowledge of the effect comes knowledge of the absolute cause, not knowledge of the specific cause. Knowledge of the principles of action is from action with the hand... This contingent is by itself [necessary?]... Then if you look at it as knowledge from where it exists, it necessarily exists... This is the desired. And the opponent might say: The contingent is true of itself... And the requirement of that is that from looking at it as it is in itself, necessity arises, but from looking at it as it is described, necessity arises. If so, we say: Looking at the contingent as it is requires that its existence or non-existence be known, but there is no impossibility in obtaining knowledge of that requirement... If knowledge of that essence does not require knowledge of that requirement, how then does the proof occur? I said: [The objector] you, the proponents, establish the indication of negation... This is the poetry. The truth is that whoever knows that A necessitates B, then B is necessary... For the same correctness of its meaning does not prevent sharing, and its being from A does not prevent it. And the universal qualified by the universal is also universal. Thus the effect known through its cause is universal. And the proponent says: this water if it is given to us from A on B... the world... we do not care about... after the A on this specific... for that is the desired. And your speech is not valid for it... Rather, the answer is: because individuals insofar as they are individuals are effects and dependent on the chain of need to the Necessary Existent. And knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect according to you. So whoever knows the Necessary Existent by essence knows individuals... from where they are temporal individuals, not from where they are universal. And that necessitates that... the desired is that... the warring things know what is suitable... the correct answer is that learning things is [dependent on] the intellect.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about causality and knowledge. The author first states that knowledge of a specific effect does not lead to knowledge of the specific cause, but only to the absolute cause. Then he presents an objection from an opponent who argues that the contingent, in itself, requires knowledge of its cause. The author responds by distinguishing between universal and particular knowledge. He argues that the effect known through its cause is universal because the cause is universal. Even if the cause is specific, the knowledge remains universal because the universal qualified by the universal is still universal. The opponent's claim that knowledge of the cause gives knowledge of the specific effect is rejected because individuals are effects in the chain of dependence on the Necessary Existent, and knowledge of the Necessary Existent by essence is of individuals only as temporal, not as universal. The passage uses examples like 'A necessitates B' to illustrate the logical point.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of an effect gives only universal knowledge of its cause, not specific knowledge.

Universal and Particular Knowledge and the Intellect
Author distinguishes universals and particulars, then defines intellect types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal and particular knowledge as described by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, and identify the characteristics of active and passive intellect.

Core Idea
Universals are stable; particulars change—so knowledge of them must differ.

In this passage, al-Razi tackles a foundational question: how can we have certain knowledge if the world changes? He distinguishes between universal natures, which are immutable, and particular things, which are mutable. This leads to a discussion of the active and passive intellect, key concepts in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of natures
Find the terms 'طبائع كلية' (universal natures) and 'شخصيات' (particulars). Note the author's claim that universals cannot change, while particulars can.
This is the core distinction driving the whole discussion.
2
Understand the consequence for knowledge
Since universals are immutable, knowledge of them is stable. Since particulars change, knowledge of them must change accordingly. This is the key epistemological point.
3
Analyze the example of future entry
The author gives an example: if someone knows that tomorrow he will enter a house, that knowledge is different from the knowledge he has when actually entering. If they were the same, one would know the entry even if he forgot the future knowledge—which is false. This refutes the identity of future and present knowledge.
The text is corrupted but the logical structure is clear.
4
Define active and passive intellect
Active intellect ('aql faʿlī) is when knowledge of a benefit causes an action. Passive intellect ('aql infiʿālī) is the capacity to receive intelligibles, like conceptualizing a building's form. Al-Razi also mentions degrees: potential intellect ('aql hayūlānī) and intellect as disposition ('aql bi-l-malaka).
These are technical terms in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ان الطبائع الكلية لما امتنع تغيرها ما هي عليه لاحرم استحال لعبة العلم بها واما الشخصيات لما صح تطرق التغير اليها ملاحهم وحب تغير العلم عند تعبرها فى انه ليس العلم بات التي سيتوجد نفس العلم بوجوده عند وجوده الذي مر كاف فيه والدي ينيك انه لو كان كذلك كان من علم أنه اذاجاء الغد دخلت يد الدار علم لامحالة دخولة التارعند على المفيد سواء علم محي الغدا ولم تعلم لكن الثاني باطل فالمقدم مثله ولان العلميات الله سيوجد استوقف كون كذلك على وجود التى ويتوقف كونه علما بوجوده على طول والحاصل قبل حصول الشرط عن الموقوف عل حصوله فالعلم بان الله سيوجد الراين بابت است مغاير العلم بوجود وفي العقل الفعلى والانفعالي العقد المعلى هو ما اذا علمت ان لكرة الفعل العلاني مصلحة فيصير ذلك العلم ما لان يوجد ذلك الله والانتعالى كمال ذا شاهدت بناء فتعقلت كيفيتمع الصين العقل للإنسان عقل على وهو مقول بالاشتراك على القوة التي بها يكون التميزين الأمور الجنة و الفتيحة وعلى المقدمات التى منها تتنط الأمة الحسنة والفتحة وبعد الاسماعية والفتيحة وعقل فظى وهو مقول كا السير الناة بالاشتراك على الجوهر المستعد لقبول التعقلات وعلى مرات أحوالها مع هذه التعقلات واول تلك المراتب ان الفصل فيها من التعقلات بالفعلق حسين يسمى عقلا هى انا وثانيها ان تحد با مهار اقليات وحفيد التي عقلا بالملكة التي حملتها بسبب تلك الاقليات ملكة الانتقال إلى الحيات ثم ان النفس ان تزت عن غيرها لكثرة هذه الأوليات وسرعة انتقالها
Then, since universal natures are impossible to change from what they are, it is impossible for knowledge of them to change. As for individual characteristics, since change is possible for them, it is necessary that knowledge changes when they change, in that it is not the same knowledge that existed when it existed... If it were so, then someone who knows that when tomorrow comes he will enter the house would necessarily know his entry at the time of entry, whether he knows tomorrow or not. But the second is false, so the premise is likewise false. And because the knowledge that God will exist is conditional on existence, and its being knowledge of His existence is conditional on the duration, and the result before the condition is the conditional's result. So knowledge that God will exist is different from knowledge of existence. And regarding the active and passive intellect: The active contract is when you know that the action has a benefit, so that knowledge becomes a cause for that action to exist... The passive is when you see a building and then conceptualize its quality... The intellect for man is called intellect by way of homonymy: it is said of the power by which one distinguishes good and bad things and of the premises from which good and bad sciences are composed, and after the sensory and imaginative. And there is a passive intellect, which is said by way of homonymy of the substance ready to receive intelligibles and of the states with these intelligibles. The first of these degrees is that the separation of intelligibles from matter is called 'intellect as potential'. The second is that you acquire the primary intelligibles and [it is] called 'intellect as disposition' because through those primary intelligibles one acquires the disposition to transition to secondary intelligibles. Then when the soul abstracts from matter due to the abundance of these primary intelligibles and the speed of transition...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a twofold discussion. First, it addresses the nature of knowledge: universals are immutable, so knowledge of them is stable; particulars are mutable, so knowledge of them must change. The author gives an example: if knowledge of a future event (entering a house tomorrow) were the same as knowledge at the time of entry, then one would know the entry at that time even if one forgot the future knowledge—which is false, so the initial assumption is false. This leads to a conclusion that knowledge of future existence is distinct from knowledge of present existence. Second, the author defines types of intellect: active intellect ('aql faʿlī) and passive intellect ('aql infiʿālī). Active intellect is described as knowledge that causes an action. Passive intellect is the receptive capacity. Then intellect is said to be used homonymously: it can refer to the power of discrimination, the premises of knowledge, or the substance that receives intelligibles. Degrees of intellect are mentioned: potential intellect ('aql hayūlānī) which is pure potentiality, and intellect as disposition ('aql bi-l-malaka) which is the acquisition of primary intelligibles enabling transition to secondary ones. Finally, the soul abstracts from matter through repetition and speed.
