Parsing the Internal Faculties
Author enumerates the four internal faculties and the dispositive faculty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each internal faculty and its function according to the classification in this passage.

Core Idea
Internal faculties are defined by their objects (forms/meanings) and operations (reception/storage/disposition).

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's Mulakhkhas presents a standard Avicennan classification of the internal perceptive faculties. Parsing this taxonomy is essential for understanding how the Islamic psychological tradition distinguishes between sensory, cognitive, and imaginative powers. Let's break down the divisions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first division
Notice the passage begins by dividing the perceptive faculty into perceiver of particulars and perceiver of universals. The universals are left aside; focus is on particulars.
This shows the author is only discussing the lower, sensory faculties here.
2
Subdivide the internal perceiver of particulars
The internal perceiver is split into two: one that merely receives without disposing (first) and one that disposes (al-quwwa al-mutasarrafa). The first is further divided by what it perceives: forms or meanings.
Forms are sensible qualities; meanings are non-material aspects like 'enmity'.
3
Match each faculty to its role
Common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) receives forms; estimation (al-wahm) receives meanings. Each has a treasury: imagination (al-khayal) stores forms; memory (al-hafiza) stores meanings.
These four are the core internal senses.
4
Examine the dispositive faculty
The fifth faculty disposes by composing and analyzing stored perceptions. Its name changes based on whether it is used by estimation (mutakhayyila) or reason (mufakkira).
This faculty is not a separate perceiver but an operator on stored content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا القوة المدركة إما أن تكون مدركة للجزئيات أو للكليات. والمدركة للجزئيات إما الظاهر وقد علم، وإما الباطن وهو ما يكون قد حصل فقط أو متصرفًا فيه. والأول إما أن يكون مدركًا للصور الجزئية أو المعاني الجزئية. فالمدرك للصور الجزئية هو الحس المشترك وهو مجمع الصور المحسوسة الظاهرة كلها. والمدرك للمعاني الجزئية هو الوهم. وخزانة الحس المشترك هي الخيال الحافظة للصور. وخزانة الوهم هي الحافظة للمعاني. فهذه أربع قوى. وأما القوة المتصرفة فهي التي من شأنها أن تصرف في المدركات المخزونة في الخزانتين بالتركيب والتحليل، وهذه إن استعملها الوهم تسمى متخيلة، وإن استعملها العقل تسمى ملكة.
They said: The perceptive faculty is either perceiving particulars or universals. The perceiver of particulars is either the external, which is known, or the internal, which is either merely having (perception) or being dispositive. The first is either perceiving particular forms or particular meanings. The perceiver of particular forms is the common sense, which is the gathering place of all apparent sensible forms. The perceiver of particular meanings is the estimative faculty. The treasury of the common sense is the imagination, which preserves forms. The treasury of the estimative faculty is the memory, which preserves meanings. These are four powers. As for the dispositive faculty, it is that whose function is to dispose of the stored perceptions in the two treasuries by composition and analysis, and if the estimative faculty uses it, it is called imaginative, and if the rational faculty uses it, it is called cogitative.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage defines the internal faculties. It begins by dividing the perceptive faculty into that which perceives particulars and that which perceives universals. The perceiver of particulars is then divided into external (the five senses, already known) and internal. The internal is subdivided into two: one that merely receives without disposing, and one that disposes. The first is further divided into two: perceiver of particular forms (common sense) and perceiver of particular meanings (estimation). Each has a treasury: imagination for forms, memory for meanings. Thus four powers: common sense, imagination, estimation, memory. Then a fifth, the dispositive faculty, which operates by composition and analysis; it is named differently depending on whether it is used by estimation (imaginative) or reason (cogitative). This classification is standard in Islamic psychology.

Key Terms

الوهم
الخيال
الحافظة
القوة المتصرفة

Key Takeaway
The internal faculties are classified by whether they perceive forms or meanings and whether they receive, store, or dispose of perceptions.

Distinguishing Imaginative and Retentive Faculties
Author argues for the distinction of the imaginative faculty from the retentive and estimative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the argument for distinguishing the imaginative and retentive faculties and explain why a single faculty cannot both perceive and transform.

Core Idea
A single faculty cannot both preserve and actively transform; thus imagination and memory must be distinct.

In Islamic psychology, the internal senses are carefully distinguished. This passage presents a key argument: the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-mutasarifa) actively combines and separates images, while the retentive faculty (al-quwwa al-hafiza) merely stores them. By the principle that a single faculty cannot be both perceptive and active, they must be distinct. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping later discussions on the soul's unity and the nature of cognition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two faculties
The passage mentions the imaginative (active) faculty and the retentive faculty. Note their Arabic names and functions.
The imaginative faculty is also called al-quwwa al-mutasarifa; the retentive is al-quwwa al-hafiza.
2
Understand the active role of imagination
The imaginative faculty combines (tarkib) and separates (tahlil) images. This is an active operation, not passive retention.
3
Grasp the principle
The author argues that a single faculty cannot be both perceptive (retaining) and active (transforming). Activity without knowledge is impossible.
This is a crucial logical principle in psychology.
4
See the refutation
An opponent might claim that a single faculty could be both, but the author responds that an active faculty must be composite, so the opponent's claim is invalid.
The text says: 'the faculty that is active must be composite' — implying that if it were simple, it could not transform.
5
Distinguish the estimative faculty
The estimative faculty (wahm) perceives non-sensible particular meanings like enmity. This further shows the need for a distinct faculty beyond imagination and memory.
Example: recognizing hostility in a person's face — not a color or shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاما القوة المتصرفة وهي الخيالية فقد احتجوا على مغايرتها للقوى المذكورة بأنها تتركب الصور الخيالية تارة وتخللها أخرى وهذا التصرف ليس للقوة المملكة فان القوة الواحدة لا تكون مدركة ومتصرفة في غير العلوم محال. ثم قالوا القوة التي نعم أنها متصرفة لابد وأن تكون مركبة وذلك بطل قولكم القوة الواحدة لا تكون مدركة ومتصرفة. وأما القوة الوهمية فقد احتجوا على مغايرتها بأنها تدرك معاني جزئية غير محسوسة كالعداوة والصداقة والبرد والحر.
As for the active faculty, which is the imaginative, they argued for its distinction from the mentioned faculties by saying that it combines imaginary forms sometimes and separates them at other times. This action does not belong to the retentive faculty, for a single faculty cannot be both perceptive and active; activity without knowledge is impossible. Then they said: The faculty that is active must be composite, so your statement that a single faculty cannot be both perceptive and active is invalid. As for the estimative faculty, they argued for its distinction by saying that it perceives particular non-sensible meanings, such as enmity, friendship, cold, and heat.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage presents arguments for the distinction between the imaginative (or active) faculty and the retentive (memory) faculty in the context of the internal senses. The author explains that the imaginative faculty actively combines and separates images, which requires a power different from mere retention. The argument is that a single faculty cannot simultaneously be receptive (perceptive) and active (transformative), because activity without knowledge is impossible. Therefore, the active faculty must be composite (i.e., not simple). This refutes the claim that a single faculty could do both. Then the estimative faculty (wahm) is distinguished by its perception of non-sensible particular meanings, like enmity and friendship, which are not perceived by the external senses or imagination. This matches the standard Avicennan classification of internal senses: common sense, imagination (retentive and active), estimation, and memory. The example used is from everyday experience: we recognize hostility in a person's demeanor, which is not a color or shape.

القوة الحافظة
القوة الوهمية

Proof that Animals Perceive Universals
Author argues that animals possess souls that perceive universals, using three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism used to prove that animals perceive universals, and identify the three supporting arguments.

Core Idea
Animals perceive universals because their intentional actions require universal concepts.

How do we know that animals, which lack rational souls, still perceive universal ideas? The author presents a categorical syllogism whose minor premise is defended by three proofs: perception of species, knowledge of particulars presupposing universals, and intention of absolute motion. This lesson will guide you through the logical structure and each proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim and syllogism
The overall argument is a categorical syllogism: Major premise: whoever perceives universals has a soul. Minor premise: animals perceive universals. Conclusion: animals have souls. The passage focuses on proving the minor premise.
The major premise is assumed from earlier context.
2
Examine the first proof: perception of species
First argument: animals perceive species (e.g., grass as a kind of food). Since a species is a universal, perceiving a species means perceiving a universal.
This proof relies on the principle that species is a universal under a genus.
3
Examine the second proof: knowledge of the particular
Second argument: knowledge of a particular, like 'this man,' is preceded by knowledge of the universal 'man.' Since animals know individual things, they must know the corresponding universal.
Logical principle: knowledge of a composite requires knowledge of its parts.
4
Examine the third proof: intention of absolute motion
Third argument: when an animal intends to move, it cannot intend a specific motion that does not yet exist (circularity). So it intends absolute motion (e.g., 'to go'), which is a universal concept.
This proof shows that intentional action presupposes universal intention.
5
Synthesize the proofs
All three proofs converge: animals' perception of species, knowledge of individuals, and goal-directed actions involve universal concepts. Therefore, the minor premise 'animals perceive universals' is established.

مباحث [في أنفس الحيوانات] [؟] منهم من [أثبتها] ومنهم من [أنكرها] ومنهم من [اقتصر على النفس العاقلة] [؟] والمثبتون [أن] الحيوانات مدركة للكليات. وكل من كان [قادراً على كذا] فله نفس. بيان الصغرى من وجوه ثلاثة: الأول: [أنها مدركة للجنيات] [؟] وكل من أدرك الجنس فقد أدرك الكلي. الثاني: [أن الإنسان جزء من هذا الإنسان] والعلم بالمركب مسبوق بالعلم بالمفرد، فالعلم بهذا الإنسان مسبوق بالعلم بالإنسان، فالعلم بالجزي مسبوق بالعلم بالكلي. الثالث: [الحيوان إذا قصد فعل الحركة] فإما أن يكون قصده متعلقاً بإيجاد الحركة أو إيجاد هذه الحركة. [والثاني باطل] لأن القصد [متعلق] بالحركة والحركة [متأخرة]، فيلزم تأخر كل منهما عن الآخر، وهو محال. فتعين الأول، وهو تعلق القصد بإيجاد الحركة [المطلقة]، وذلك يستلزم إدراك الكلي. فوجب أن تكون الحيوانات مدركة للكليات. [لأن كل نوع من الحيوان طالب لنوع معين من الغذاء] فلولا أنها تميز النوع المطلوب عن النوع المكروه لا يفعل ذلك.
Chapter on [the souls of animals] [؟] Some of them [affirm them] and some [deny them] and some [limit to rational soul] [؟]. And those who affirm [that] animals perceive universals. And everyone who is [capable of such] has a soul. Explanation of the minor premise from three aspects: First: [that they perceive species] [؟] and whoever perceives the genus has perceived the universal. Second: [that man is part of this man] and knowledge of the composite is preceded by knowledge of the part, so knowledge of this man is preceded by knowledge of man, so knowledge of the particular is preceded by knowledge of the universal. Third: [the animal when it intends to produce motion] either its intention is attached to producing motion or to producing this motion. [And the second is invalid] because the intention [is attached] to the motion and the motion [is subsequent], so each would be delayed from the other, which is impossible. So the first is determined, which is the attachment of intention to producing [absolute] motion, and that necessitates perception of the universal. So it is necessary that animals perceive universals. [because each type of animal seeks a specific type of food] and if it did not distinguish the desired type from the disliked type, it would not do that.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative text that aims to prove that animals have souls that perceive universals, not just particulars. The author begins by noting that scholars differ on whether animals have souls or not, with some limiting souls to humans. The author sides with those who affirm that animals perceive universals. The proof is structured as a categorical syllogism: the minor premise is that animals capable of certain actions have souls; the major premise is that those actions require perception of universals. The minor premise is explained through three arguments. First, animals perceive species (e.g., grass as grass), and perceiving a species means perceiving a universal. Second, knowledge of a particular, like 'this man,' presupposes knowledge of the universal 'man,' since the particular is part of the universal. Third, when an animal intends to perform an action, it cannot intend a specific motion that does not yet exist; rather, it must intend an absolute motion, which involves a universal concept. Thus, animals must perceive universals. The author concludes by giving an example: each animal species seeks a specific type of food and avoids harmful ones, which requires distinguishing the universal kind, not just individual instances.

Key Terms
الكلي
النفس

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does perceiving a species imply perceiving a universal?
A) Because species are particular instances of a universal.
B) Because a species is itself a universal (a kind under a genus).
C) Because all animals perceive universals innately.
D) Because the author defines species as universal.

Key Takeaway
The three proofs collectively demonstrate that animals' goal-directed actions and perception require universal concepts, thus they have souls that perceive universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism from the three proofs, identifying the major and minor premises and the conclusion.
?
Apply the third argument (intention of absolute motion) to a new example: a bird building a nest. Explain how the bird's intention must involve a universal concept.

The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Proofs from Movement and Composition
First two proofs for animal perception of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the arguments from movement and composition that animals perceive universals.

Core Idea
Movement and compositional knowledge imply awareness of universals.

The author presents two concise proofs: animals move correctly towards unseen objects, and knowledge of a particular man requires prior knowledge of man in general. We will analyze their logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching claim
The author states that animals perceive particulars, and whoever perceives a particular has perceived a universal. This is the conclusion he wants to prove.
The claim: 'كل من أدرك الجزئي فقد أدرك الكلي'
2
Extract the first proof (movement)
The first proof is simply 'صحة الحركة' – the correctness of movement. Consider why a hungry animal moves towards herb it does not yet see. This shows it knows the universal 'herb'.
This is a compressed argument; we must unpack it.
3
Extract the second proof (composition)
The author argues: 'Man is a part of this man; knowledge of the composite (this man) is preceded by knowledge of its parts (man). Therefore knowledge of this man precedes knowledge of man, which is a universal.'
Formalize as: Major: Knowledge of composite requires knowledge of part. Minor: Man is part of this man. Conclusion: Knowledge of this man requires knowledge of man (universal).
4
Combine the proofs
Both proofs show that animals must have awareness of universals to perform actions like seeking food or recognizing individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الصغرى من وجوه ثلثة: فإنها مدركة للجزئيات وكل من أدرك الجزئي فقد أدرك الكلي. أما الأول: فصحة الحركة. وأما الثاني: فإن الإنسان جزء من هذا الإنسان والعلم بالمركب مسبوق بالعلم بمفرداته. فالعلم بهذا الإنسان مسبوق بالعلم بالإنسان، فالعلم بالإنسان مسبوق بالعلم بالكلي. وأما الثالث: فإن الحيوان إذا قصد فعل الحركة فإما أن يكون قصده متعلقا بإيجاد الحركة مطلقا أو بإيجاد هذه الحركة المعينة. الأول باطل لأن القصد المتعلق بهذه الحركة ينافي تعلق القصد بها بعد تحققها. والثاني يلزم منه تعلق القصد بها قبل تحققها، وهو محال. فثبت أن القصد متعلق بإيجاد شخص الحركة لا بإيجاد هذه الحركة. وإذا سلم تعلق القصد بالشخص، لزم الشعور بالكلي. فوجب أن تكون الحيوانات مدركة للكليات، إذ كل نوع من الحيوان طالب لنوع معين من الغذاء وكاره لنوع آخر. فلو لا أنها تعلم النوع المطلوب عن النوع المكروه لما أمكن ذلك. فإن قيل: الحيوان لا شعور له بالماهيات النوعية، بل إذا شاهد عشبا معينا أراده، وإذا شاهد عظما معينا كرهه. قلنا: هذا يخلاف الحس، فإنها إذا جاعت تطلب العشب وإن لم تشاهد عينا، وإذا شاهدت عظما خافت وإن لم تشاهد أسدا. فالعشب من حيث هو عشب هو المطلوب، والعظم من حيث هو عظم هو المكروه.
Explanation of the minor premise from three aspects: Indeed, it (the animal) perceives particulars, and everyone who perceives a particular has perceived a universal. As for the first: the correctness of movement. As for the second: Man is a part of this man, and knowledge of the composite is preceded by knowledge of its components. So knowledge of this man is preceded by knowledge of man, and knowledge of man is preceded by knowledge of the universal. As for the third: When an animal intends to perform a movement, either its intention is related to bringing about movement absolutely, or to bringing about this specific movement. The first is invalid because the intention related to this specific movement precludes the intention relating to it after its actualization. The second entails the intention relating to it before its actualization, which is impossible. So it is established that the intention is related to bringing about a particular movement, not this specific movement. If the intention related to the particular is granted, then awareness of the universal follows. So it is necessary that animals perceive universals, since every species of animal seeks a specific type of food and dislikes another type. Thus if it did not know the desired type from the disliked type, that would not be possible. If it is said: The animal has no awareness of specific natures; rather, when it sees a specific herb it wants it, and when it sees a specific bone it dislikes it. We say: This contradicts sense, for when it is hungry it seeks herb even if it has not seen a specific one, and when it sees a bone it fears even if it has not seen a lion. So herb qua herb is what is desired, and bone qua bone is what is disliked.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs for the minor premise of a syllogism concluding that animals perceive universals. The author is arguing that animals must have awareness of universal concepts because their behaviors—seeking specific types of food and fearing specific predators—cannot be explained by mere perception of particulars. The first proof is from the correctness of movement: animals move towards objects they have not yet perceived, implying knowledge of the universal type. The second proof uses the example of man: knowing an individual man requires prior knowledge of the universal 'man'. The third proof is about intention: when an animal intends to move, it cannot intend an absolutely universal movement nor a fully determined particular movement; rather, it intends a specific movement undetermined as to its individual instance, which requires knowledge of the universal type. The author then addresses an objection that animals respond only to particular percepts. He refutes it by noting that hungry animals seek herb in general, and fear bone even without seeing a lion, proving they recognize the universal types.

Key Terms
جزئي (juz'ī): A particular individual instance, e.g., a specific herb or bone.
كلي (kullī): A universal concept shared by many particulars, e.g., 'herb' as a type. [Note: In this text, kullī is used to mean the universal form that animals perceive, not merely a logical term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the composition proof, why must knowledge of 'this man' be preceded by knowledge of 'man'?
A) Because 'man' is a species and 'this man' is an individual.
B) Because 'man' is a component of the composite 'this man'.
C) Because animals cannot know individuals directly.
D) Because the author wants to prove animals are rational.

Key Takeaway
The movement and composition proofs show that animals must have universal concepts to explain their correct actions and recognition of individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the first proof from movement as a syllogism. Use Islamic examples: 'Every thirsty animal seeks water. This horse is thirsty. Therefore this horse seeks water.' Does this prove the horse has a universal concept of water? Explain.
?
Given the second proof, fill in the blanks: 'Knowledge of this man requires knowledge of ____. Man is a ____ of this man. Therefore knowledge of this man requires knowledge of the universal ____.'
